Importance: ***

Reuters Data

S&P Global Manufacturing PMI SA: 53.3 | Prior: 50.0

Professional Market Commentary

S&P Global reported a notable April upturn in Canadian manufacturing, with output and new orders rising at rates not seen since the first half of 2022, supported by stockbuilding. The release also emphasized that the Middle East war produced supply disruption and steeply rising costs, with firms raising employment and purchasing while hoping growth would continue. (pmi.spglobal.com
)

Comment — Economist Layer

This confirms the Canada manufacturing upside leg.

The release fits the BoC/GDP sequence: February GDP showed manufacturing strength, the BoC upgraded growth slightly, and April PMI now confirms that manufacturing momentum persisted into Q2. The issue is that the driver is partly stockbuilding and product-availability hedging, so this is not pure demand strength.

Canada differs from the UK and Australia in one important respect: business optimism improved rather than collapsed. That makes Canada the cleaner domestic-resilience case within the DM frontloading basket.

State transition: continuation

Context from prior regional live file: Canada had already moved into a two-sided stagflation regime, with energy inflation risk and trade-policy downside both live. Manufacturing strength keeps the growth side firmer than feared. It does not remove BoC caution, but it supports the market’s H2 hike-risk lean if April CPI confirms pass-through.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

CAD-positive at the margin and supportive of BoC hike optionality.

The print strengthens the case that Canada can absorb some inflation pressure without immediate growth failure. But the policy path still hinges on whether input-cost and energy pressure move into core CPI, and whether CUSMA/tariff risk damages the export cycle later.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: DM current activity looks better than feared because frontloading and stockbuilding are lifting PMIs, but the same releases show weaker quality through delivery delays, cost pressure, lower optimism and future payback risk.

Why it matters: Central banks cannot treat the activity resilience as clean growth, but they also cannot ignore the inflation pass-through risk embedded in supplier delays and input costs.

Implication: The correct DM frame is not broad easing or broad tightening; it is conditional holds with asymmetric hike risk where second-round effects validate, and later growth payback risk where frontloading fades.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

Australia CPI / Trimmed Mean CPI / Weighted Median CPI (29 Apr)
Why it matters: Highest inflation-policy signal of the week. Headline inflation re-accelerated sharply on fuel, but underlying inflation did not yet break, making the RBA decision a live hike-versus-hawkish-hold problem.
United Kingdom BOE Bank Rate / MPC Vote + UK PMI / Credit Sequence (30 Apr–1 May)
Why it matters: The BoE formalized the active-hold regime, then the following day’s PMI and credit data showed more resilience and cost pressure than a simple demand-break story. This is the cleanest BoE second-round-risk setup.
Japan BOJ Decision + Tokyo CPI / Manufacturing PMI Sequence (28 Apr–1 May)
Why it matters: The BOJ hold with three hike dissents remains the Japan anchor. Tokyo CPI looks soft but subsidy-distorted, while PMI shows frontloaded activity and supply-chain stress. Japan is not cleanly weak; it is policy-constrained through inflation, FX and supply disruption.

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 20 Apr 2026 to 24 Apr 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

DM ended the week in a stagflationary headline-versus-composition regime. Current activity prints often looked better than feared — UK PMIs and retail sales beat, Japan manufacturing surged, Canada retail remained resilient, and New Zealand trade improved tactically — but the internals repeatedly showed front-loading, delivery-time distortions, fuel stockpiling, weak forward confidence, softer services demand, and fading leading indicators. The growth signal was therefore not recessionary, but lower quality: resilient enough to block easy easing narratives, not clean enough to validate a durable reacceleration.

The inflation and policy signal was more decisive. New Zealand CPI was the cleanest hawkish print; UK CPI was less broad than the headline but pipeline PPI and PMI input costs escalated sharply; Canada CPI remained look-throughable at the core level but PPI reopened pass-through risk; Japan CPI and especially service PPI strengthened the BoJ normalization case. Labor did not deliver a broad DM break, but UK internals softened beneath a false-positive unemployment headline. The resulting regime is policy-constrained divergence: RBNZ constraint rises, BoC remains in hard hold, BoE stays trapped, BoJ holds with a harder June path, and RBA remains squeezed between softer forward growth and hotter costs.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Headline growth optics were better than the underlying tape: UK flash PMIs beat strongly and retail sales rose 0.7% m/m, but both were flattered by front-loading, delivery-time effects and fuel stockpiling. Japan manufacturing PMI jumped to 54.9, but S&P Global tied the move to supply-disruption hedging while services slowed. Canada retail remained resilient but volume growth was only 0.3%, and Australia’s leading index slipped below trend. The week therefore avoided a clean growth-break signal but did not validate durable reacceleration.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
UK unemployment fell to 4.9%, but the live file treated that as a false dawn because payrolls were negative, vacancies fell, inactivity rose, and private-sector regular pay slowed. Sweden’s NSA unemployment rise was discounted by the official smoothed seasonally adjusted trend, while Norway LFS was unchanged at 4.9%. Labor softened at the UK margin but did not produce a synchronized DM deterioration.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
Inflation risk deteriorated across the file even where core consumer data were less alarming. New Zealand CPI beat consensus and non-tradables stayed firm at 3.5% y/y. UK CPI was compositionally less threatening than headline, but input PPI surged 4.4% m/m and PMI input costs hit record pressure. Canada CPI remained look-throughable at core level, but producer prices jumped 2.4% m/m and raw-material prices rose 12.0% m/m. Japan core CPI rose to 1.8% and service PPI accelerated to 3.1%, hardening the BoJ inflation channel.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
The clearest financial-conditions channel was the UK housing and confidence transmission. Rightmove explicitly linked weaker annual house-price momentum to the mortgage-rate backup, with the average two-year fixed rate at 5.42% versus 4.25% before the shock. UK consumer confidence fell to -25, business optimism collapsed to -65, and producer-input shocks implied margin squeeze before full consumer pass-through. Conditions were not uniformly tight across all DM economies, but the week showed the shock tightening the private-sector constraint through rates, costs, confidence and margins.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The policy constraint rose because the week delivered inflation pressure without clean demand strength. New Zealand CPI materially hardened the RBNZ tightening-contingency story; the UK mix kept the BoE trapped between softer labor/forward demand and higher pipeline inflation; Canada preserved an April BoC hold but made pass-through monitoring more urgent after PPI; Japan CPI and service PPI supported an April hold with hawkish guidance and a live June normalization debate; Australia’s PMI and leading index kept the RBA in hold-with-hawkish-bias rather than a clean hike or easing regime.

Stats NZ highlighted stronger March goods exports and imports, with exports up 7.3% y/y to $7.9bn. Public coverage focused on precious metals, jewellery and coins, plus fruit, as the main contributors, which made the monthly surplus look solid but compositionally narrow rather than broad-based.

The monthly swing is undeniably positive, but the hierarchy is still mixed. The surplus widened sharply because exports accelerated, yet imports also rose strongly and the annual trade balance deteriorated further to -$3.19bn. That means the external picture improved on the month without repairing the broader rolling trend. The composition also matters: fruit seasonality and precious-metals strength are not the same as a generalized export reacceleration. State transition: no material change.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM file had New Zealand already splitting between weak domestic services demand and somewhat better external and rural-income support. This March trade print fits that same split rather than overturning it. The external side improves tactically, but the NZ macro regime still hinges much more on inflation and domestic activity than on one strong monthly surplus.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Helpful for NZD at the margin, but secondary for the RBNZ. The strategist relevance is mostly cushioning: firmer trade softens the growth downside a little, yet it does not change the policy story unless later data show that external income support is broadening into domestic demand.
United Kingdom — House Price Rightmove MM / YY (Apr)
Release Date: 20 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 01:01
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

House Price Rightmove MM: 0.8% | Prior: 0.8%
House Price Rightmove YY: -0.9% | Prior: -0.2%

Professional Market Commentary

Rightmove framed the April release as a steady but clearly rate-constrained spring market. Asking prices rose 0.8% m/m to £373,971, but that was below the long-run April average of 1.2%; homes for sale were at an eleven-year high, buyers had the most choice for any April in the past decade, and the average two-year fixed mortgage rate had risen to 5.42% from 4.25% before the Iran-war shock.

Comment — Economist Layer

The relevant signal is not the positive monthly move but the annual deterioration and its transmission mechanism. A typical spring rise still occurred, yet it was muted, the annual rate fell deeper into negative territory, and the official commentary tied that weakness directly to higher mortgage rates rather than to an outright collapse in transaction activity. This therefore looks like a clean cost-of-credit transmission print: the housing channel is weakening because financing costs have backed up, even though the market has not yet broken on volumes. State transition: deterioration.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM file already had UK housing as subdued rather than collapsing, with Nationwide and Halifax showing only modest resilience while construction stayed weak. April extends that story in a more clearly negative direction. Housing remains functional, but the post-shock mortgage-rate backup is now visibly weighing on pricing momentum.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This reinforces the BoE trap rather than changing the BoE path. The housing channel is weakening exactly where cuts would normally help, but imported inflation risk keeps policy from easing that pressure. Strategically, it is another reason why UK growth-sensitive assets should not over-read isolated monthly activity beats.

This is a classic false-dawn PMI. The rebound above 50 looks better, but the important internals are not genuinely strong: manufacturing output remained below 50, new business fell again, and employment in manufacturing deteriorated, while input-cost inflation accelerated sharply. So the signal is not “Australia recovered,” but “headline PMI arithmetic improved while real demand remained weak and price pressure worsened.” That matters because it fits the leading-index deterioration from the day before. State transition: false dawn.
Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM file had Australia as the main hawkish outlier because labour remained resilient. This PMI release tempers that picture. It does not negate Australia’s inflation constraint, but it weakens the case that activity is firm enough to absorb further tightening cleanly.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The RBA gets ammunition for both sides. Hawks can point to hot input costs; doves can point to weak demand and contracting manufacturing output. Net, this still looks more like hold-with-hawkish-bias than a clean hike signal.

Canada — Producer Prices MM (Mar)
Release Date: 23 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Producer Prices MM: 2.4% | Prior: 0.4% | Revised: 0.6% | Poll: 1.9% | Surprise: 0.50%

Professional Market Commentary

Statistics Canada reported a large producer-price jump, with industrial product prices up 2.4% m/m and raw-material prices up 12.0% m/m. Refined petroleum products dominated, but chemicals, plastic resins and fertilizers also rose, which made the producer-side shock broader than a pure gasoline story even though energy still explained most of it.

This is where the Canadian “look-through” CPI story gets tested. HF Take — Strategist Layer

This does not overturn the April 29 hold. But it does make the May CPI release much more important, because the gap between producer inflation and consumer core now has to be monitored as a live transmission risk rather than as a hypothetical.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK
Main contradiction: DM headline data often looked stronger or hotter than the underlying regime, but the internals repeatedly pointed to front-loading, delivery-time distortions, margin squeeze, and weakening forward confidence.
Why it matters: A superficial reading would have called this a week of stronger UK and Japan activity plus hotter DM inflation. The composition says something else: the shock is propagating through distortions and cost channels rather than through durable domestic-strength channels.
Implication: Policy divergence widens further. RBNZ constraint rises, BoC remains in hard hold, BoE stays trapped, BoJ holds but hardens, and RBA remains squeezed between softer growth and hotter costs.

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 13 Apr 2026 to 17 Apr 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The DM week resolved into a backward-looking strength versus current transmission split. February hard data in the UK, Japan, and parts of Canada showed that the region entered the Iran shock with more momentum than feared: UK GDP and services beat strongly, Japan machinery orders rebounded sharply, and Canadian manufacturing and wholesale activity recovered. But the more current and more policy-relevant signals were less clean. New Zealand’s services and core retail demand deteriorated, Canadian permits and housing starts confirmed a genuine construction slowdown, Sweden kept soft realized core inflation but added firmer long-run inflation expectations, and the UK’s strongest growth print was explicitly pre-war. Australia was the main current-data hawkish outlier through labour-market resilience. The resulting regime is not synchronized collapse and not synchronized reflation, but a bidirectional DM tape with wider policy dispersion and a higher bar for simple easing conclusions.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Japan machinery orders rose 13.6% m/m and 24.7% y/y, UK February GDP beat strongly at 0.5% m/m and 1.0% y/y, and Canadian manufacturing and wholesale sales rebounded. But New Zealand PSI fell to 46.1, Canadian building permits dropped 8.4% m/m and housing starts missed at 235.9k, and the UK strength was explicitly backward-looking pre-war data. The week showed better entry momentum than feared, but weaker current transmission.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Australia’s labour report was the clearest positive labour signal: employment rose 17.9k, full-time jobs increased sharply, and unemployment held at 4.3%. Against that, New Zealand PSI kept employment below 50, pointing to softer domestic labour conditions. The regional labour read therefore stayed uneven rather than breaking decisively weaker or re-tightening broadly.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Sweden’s realized inflation remained soft, with CPIF at 1.6% y/y and CPIF ex energy at 1.1%, confirming weak domestic core. But Sweden’s 5-year money-market CPIF expectations rose to 2.2%, New Zealand retail/card spending was flattered by fuel inflation, and UK trade remained vulnerable to a higher energy-import bill. The week did not produce broad realized reflation, but it did preserve inflation-risk channels.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
Japan M2 and broad money continued to expand, confirming accommodative domestic liquidity, and none of the week’s releases pointed to generalized DM funding stress. At the same time, housing and construction-sensitive channels weakened in Canada, and several of the best growth prints were backward-looking rather than evidence of easier current conditions. Financial conditions did not look restrictive enough to explain the slowdown outright, but they were not delivering a clean cyclical re-acceleration either.

Policy Constraint: +
Supporting evidence:
Australia preserved live RBA tightening optionality through resilient labour data; Sweden’s soft realized core was offset by firmer long-run inflation expectations; the UK kept a stagflationary trade and energy-risk profile even after a strong GDP beat; and Canada’s weak construction sequence deepened growth concerns without resolving the BoC’s inflation problem. Across the region, central banks stayed constrained by two-sided risk rather than receiving a clean easing signal.

Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Machinery Orders MM: 13.6% | Prior: -5.5% | Poll: -1.1%
Machinery Orders YY: 24.7% | Prior: 13.7% | Poll: 8.5%

Professional Market Commentary

The official Cabinet Office release confirmed a sharp rebound in core private-sector machinery orders in February. The surrounding institutional read is that capex remains one of the cleaner supports to the Japan story and that investment is not currently being choked by higher rates.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the strongest upside hard-data surprise in the file. A 13.6% m/m rebound after January’s 5.5% fall tells us the capex side of Japan’s economy was much firmer in February than consensus expected, which directly offsets the weak household-demand story and argues against reading Japan as a simple domestic slowdown case. The volatility caveat still applies, but in weekly sequence this materially strengthens one of the main pillars supporting medium-term BOJ normalization.

State transition: reversal

Context from prior regional live file:
Japan entered the week with household and consumer-sensitive indicators soft, but with parts of the corporate side still more resilient. February machinery orders now push that corporate-capex side back into clear leadership. Japan therefore remains a split economy in which investment is still cushioning softer consumption.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the cleanest pro-BOJ data point of the week because it reduces concern that business investment is already rolling over. It does not force an immediate hike call under current geopolitical uncertainty, but it supports the medium-term normalization narrative and is mildly JPY-supportive at the margin.

Sweden — Money Mkt CPIF Inflation 5 Yrs (Apr)
Release Date: 15 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 08:00
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Money Mkt CPIF Inflation 5 Yrs: 2.2% | Prior: 2.1%

Professional Market Commentary

The official Origo/Prospera survey commissioned by the Riksbank showed CPIF expectations at 2.156% for five years ahead, up from just above 2.1% in March, while one-year CPIF expectations stood materially higher at 1.737% in year 1 and CPI expectations were firmer at shorter horizons. The broad message is a modest firming in long-horizon inflation expectations even as realized March inflation remained soft.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the key Swedish policy complication of the week. Realized CPIF and CPIF ex energy were soft, but the five-year expectations horizon ticked above target, which means the Riksbank now faces a clearer tension between weak current core inflation and modestly firmer longer-run inflation psychology. The gap between soft realized core and firmer expectations is exactly what keeps Sweden from becoming a straightforward dovish case.

State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
Sweden entered the week with realized core inflation already undershooting and domestic demand still fragile. The April expectations survey does not overturn that softness, but it adds a second signal running in the other direction by nudging longer-horizon inflation expectations above target. Sweden therefore becomes a cleaner optionality dilemma rather than a simple disinflation story.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the more hawkish Sweden signal of the week and partly offsets the soft core-inflation read. It does not force a Riksbank hike narrative on its own, but it validates the Bank’s bidirectional optionality and keeps SEK downside from becoming a one-way rates story.

Canada — Manufacturing Sales MM (Feb)
Release Date: 15 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:30
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Manufacturing Sales MM: 3.6% | Prior: -3.0% | Revised: -3.1% | Poll: 3.8% | Surprise: -0.2pp

Professional Market Commentary

Statistics Canada reported a strong February rebound, with manufacturing sales up 3.6% and transportation equipment rebounding 18.8% after January’s shutdown-related weakness. The official composition also showed machinery and primary metals improving, with primary metals hitting a record high.

Comment — Economist Layer

The headline rebound is real, but the hierarchy matters. Transportation equipment is doing much of the work via plant restart and retooling normalization, so this is not a clean signal of broad structural acceleration; the more informative positive is that machinery and primary metals also improved, which suggests the February rebound was not purely automotive noise.

State transition: reversal

Context from prior regional live file:
Canada entered the week with activity already softening through Ivey and permits. February manufacturing sales offset that picture at the margin, but not enough to erase it because the rebound was still partly restart-driven and backward-looking. Canada therefore stays mixed rather than turning decisively stronger.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the BoC, this print partly offsets the weaker construction side but does not resolve the macro trade-off. It supports a complex-hold reading rather than a clean growth scare, especially because later releases still need to confirm whether the February rebound survived into March.

Together with manufacturing, this stops the Canada file from becoming a one-way growth-deterioration story. For the BoC it preserves the complex-hold regime: construction is weakening, but goods-producing and distribution activity still showed backward-looking resilience.

Professional Market Commentary

ONS reported a major February beat across the headline UK activity measures, with GDP up 0.5% m/m and 1.0% y/y, services and production both stronger, and only manufacturing remaining weak. This is the clearest false-dawn print in the file. The forward-looking message is very different, because the ONS caveat, the March flash PMI slowdown to 51.0, and the UK’s energy-import exposure all mean that this February beat cannot be extrapolated cleanly into Q2.

State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file:
The UK entered the new week with March PMIs already near stall and cost pressure worsening. February GDP offsets that weakness only in backward-looking terms because it predates the shock and leaves the forward trajectory unresolved in the wrong direction. The UK therefore looks stronger entering the shock, not stronger after it.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

In isolation the print is GBP-positive and BoE-hawkish. In the live policy frame, however, it mainly raises the starting point from which subsequent shock-related deterioration will be measured; the BoE still has to weigh energy-driven inflation risk against slowing forward demand, so the rate path remains a hold-or-hawkish-risk story rather than a clean growth-optimism story.

GBP-neutral on the day because the poll beat is overshadowed by the forward energy-import problem. Strategically, this reinforces the BoE’s uncomfortable mix: headline inflation risk can rise even while the trade and growth backdrop become less friendly.

Canada — Housing Starts, Annualized (Mar)
Release Date: 17 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:15
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Housing Starts, Annualized: 235.9k | Prior: 250.9k | Poll: 255.0k

Professional Market Commentary

CMHC’s March release was more explicit than the consensus narrative. The agency said the six-month trend fell 2.9% to 248,378, monthly SAAR starts fell 6% to 235,852 from 250,961, and Chief Economist Mathieu Laberge said the data pointed to a continued loss of momentum in housing construction, broadly in line with CMHC’s housing market outlook.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is an important confirmation print because it converts the earlier permits weakness into actual starts weakness. CMHC itself now explicitly links permitting and starts in the March release, which strengthens the sequence logic: February building permits missed badly, and March housing starts then followed with a meaningful downside miss and a drop below the recent trend. The year-on-year rise in actual starts does not offset that weekly message because CMHC also stresses that construction momentum is fading rather than improving.

State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
Canada had already entered the week with activity softening and the BoC stuck between weaker growth and still-uncomfortable inflation dynamics. March housing starts intensify the growth-side deterioration by confirming that residential construction is now following the earlier permits weakness. Canada therefore moves deeper into a weak-construction, hard-hold policy mix rather than out of it.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is more decision-relevant than the permits print because it confirms the slowdown in actual construction activity, not just intentions. For rates it leans dovish on growth, but the bigger strategic value is cumulative: together with permits, it gives the BoC a cleaner construction-softening sequence and makes it harder to argue that the spring housing cycle is rescuing the outlook.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: The best-looking hard data in the file are backward-looking pre-war prints, while the more current forward and near-real-time indicators are already softening or becoming more inflation-risky.

Why it matters: It prevents a simple mapping from strong February data into durable optimism, and it also prevents a simple mapping from softer March-April demand into easy easing.

Implication: DM remains a bidirectional, policy-dispersion tape, with Australia the clearest live hawkish outlier and Sweden the cleanest realized-inflation versus expectations dilemma.

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 7 Apr 2026 to 10 Apr 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

DM resolved into a broad shock-driven stagflation week, but with sharp internal divergence rather than a single regional macro line. Growth weakened across most rate- and demand-sensitive channels: Australia’s final PMIs confirmed broad private-sector contraction, UK services/all-sector/housing data rolled over together, Canada’s activity pulse softened materially, and Japan’s household sector stayed weak; Sweden offered only stale February stabilisation and New Zealand still held above 50 in manufacturing but with weaker direction and worse sentiment. Labor did not break cleanly, but nor did it deliver relief: Switzerland stayed steady, Canada stabilised only weakly after prior losses, and wage pressure there remained too firm. Inflation and policy tension stayed the binding issue. Japan’s CGPI, Norway’s headline CPI and the UK cost backdrop all kept the upstream-price problem alive, while Sweden was the main domestic-core exception through a sharp CPIF-XE undershoot. The resulting regime was not broad DM recession and not broad DM reflation, but a widening policy-dispersion tape under a common stagflationary shock: RBNZ moved into explicit tightening contingency, Norges Bank kept the clearest hike bias, BoJ’s debate became more explicitly stagflation-shaped, BoC’s trade-off stayed awkward, the Riksbank faced softer core, and the SNB remained in intervention mode.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
Australia’s services and composite finals stayed in contraction at 46.3 and 46.6; UK services/composite/all-sector PMIs moved to near-stall or contraction territory and housing surveys deteriorated; Canada’s Ivey SA fell to 49.7 and the leading index decelerated sharply; Japan’s household spending missed badly; Norway manufacturing stayed soft. Sweden’s February GDP/production bundle stabilised, and New Zealand manufacturing stayed above 50, but neither was strong enough to overturn the broader weakening DM activity picture.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week did not deliver a region-wide labour break. Switzerland’s adjusted unemployment rate held at 3.0%, and Canada’s March jobs report stabilised after heavy January-February losses with unemployment unchanged at 6.7%. But that stabilisation was low-quality, with part-time gains, full-time softness and wages still running 4.7% y/y. The labour signal therefore stayed mixed: not collapsing, but not easing the broader macro trade-off.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
Inflation pressure did not resolve cleanly lower across DM. Japan’s CGPI accelerated to 2.6% y/y with imported-cost pressure re-heating, Norway’s headline CPI rose to 3.6% y/y with core still firm at 3.0%, and UK survey evidence showed worsening input-cost pressure. Canada’s wage backdrop also kept second-round inflation risk alive. Sweden was the major exception, with CPIF at 1.6% and CPIF ex energy at 1.1%, but that softer domestic-core signal remained the outlier rather than the regional norm.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
The week showed tighter or more restrictive transmission rather than easing relief. UK housing data from Halifax and RICS confirmed higher mortgage rates were biting demand; the UK construction/housing mix stayed weak for financing-sensitive sectors. Switzerland stayed in active FX-management mode through rising reserves after SNB intervention willingness was emphasised, while Sweden’s softer housing-expectations survey pointed to fragile household confidence rather than a self-sustaining easing impulse. Across the region, market conditions remained shaped by the shock, not by a clean loosening cycle.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
Bad growth did not map into easy easing. The RBNZ held at 2.25% but shifted into explicit tightening-contingency language; Norges Bank’s inflation mix kept its hawkish path intact; Japan’s weak demand plus firmer CGPI made the BoJ debate more explicitly stagflation-shaped; the BoC faced weak activity but still-firm wages; the BoE’s growth slowdown came with worsening cost optics; only Sweden offered a cleaner dovish core-inflation signal, while Switzerland stayed focused on intervention rather than rate relief. The regional policy constraint therefore tightened overall even as activity softened.

The official S&P Global final confirmed that the March shock was not repaired at the final stage. Services stayed firmly in contraction, demand softened further, confidence weakened, and price pressures remained intense. The combined signal is not a one-off survey wobble but a broad activity break with stagflationary cost characteristics.

The key macro point is breadth. Australia no longer has services offsetting manufacturing weakness; both sides of the private sector are now rolling over together. That materially worsens the policy mix because the survey does not deliver clean disinflation alongside weaker output. The prior DM live file had Australia entering the shock with credit still relatively firm rather than already rolling over, especially in private-sector and housing credit. HF Take — Strategist Layer

AUD-negative on growth, but not cleanly RBA-dovish. The market can lean into weaker activity, but the central bank still faces a cost-side problem. The next verification test is whether harder domestic data start validating the PMI collapse rather than containing it.

This is SEK-negative and Riksbank-dovish at the margin, but only conditionally. The market can trade the core miss immediately; the Riksbank still has to worry about the possibility that a prolonged energy shock later spills into broader prices. Switzerland — Forex Reserves CHF (Mar)
Release Date: 7 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 09:00
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Forex Reserves: CHF 721,218M | Prior: CHF 710,029M | Revised: CHF 710,105M

Professional Market Commentary

The SNB’s March policy communication made the directional framework explicit: the policy rate stayed at 0%, but the Bank said its willingness to intervene in the foreign exchange market had increased given the Middle East conflict. In that context, a rise in reserves is institutionally meaningful rather than random balance-sheet noise.

Comment — Economist Layer

The reserve increase does not prove intervention mechanically because monthly reserve changes can include valuation effects. But in sequence the move is highly suggestive: after the SNB openly emphasised greater willingness to lean against franc strength, reserves increased rather than continued their earlier decline. That is enough to treat March as consistent with intervention-mode policy.

State transition: reversal

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had Switzerland opening with a weaker KOF signal and softer growth momentum. This reserve increase therefore matters because it shows the Swiss policy mix is not primarily responding through rates; it is responding through the exchange-rate channel while the growth backdrop remains fragile.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This keeps Switzerland in intervention mode rather than rate-hike mode. For FX, the relevant point is that safe-haven CHF appreciation is meeting an active policy response; for rates, low inflation still means the bar for tightening remains high relative to peers.

Reuters and the official S&P Global release converged on the same message: March activity slowed sharply, optimism faded, export demand deteriorated, and cost pressure accelerated. Services input prices rose to their highest level in nearly a year, with the monthly jump the biggest since 2021.

Comment — Economist Layer

The important hierarchy is that the final revision moved the UK from “slowing but maybe still okay” toward “near-stall with worsening inflation optics.” A composite of 50.3 is effectively flat, not reassuring expansion. At the same time, the price side remains the binding complication for the BoE. So this is not a simple demand-softening release that reopens easy cuts. It is a worse trade-off release: weaker activity, firmer costs, softer confidence, and weaker external demand.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had UK money-and-credit data looking firmer than feared, but explicitly as a backward-looking and potentially fragile signal. This PMI block resolves that tension the wrong way: the late-Q1 live survey now matters more than the earlier February credit resilience.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For markets, this keeps the BoE in a hawkish-hold or cuts-delayed bucket rather than reopening a clean easing story. Sterling support from rates need not disappear immediately, but the UK macro mix is becoming increasingly hostile to assets that require simultaneous growth and disinflation.

Professional Market Commentary

The headline NSA surplus beat expectations, but the broader public read remained compositionally cautious: Japan’s current account strength continues to rely heavily on its structural income surplus rather than on a strengthening goods balance.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a classic composition-over-headline release. Context from prior regional live file:

This release preserves the idea that Japan’s external surplus still exists, but it shifts the internal balance toward a less friendly interpretation because the trade leg is now visibly eroding.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not a clean JPY-bullish external-strength signal. Japan still has an income-surplus cushion, but oil-sensitive import dynamics are becoming a more serious headwind. That complicates any BoJ attempt to lean aggressively into further tightening.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: DM activity weakened further, but upstream price and wage pressure did not roll over; Sweden was the main exception on core inflation.
Why it matters: It prevents a normal “bad growth equals easier policy” mapping.
Implication: Relative-value DM policy divergence remains the dominant framing rather than a one-directional regional easing story.

New Zealand — Cash Rate (8 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This was the clearest central-bank reaction-function shift in the entire DM tape. The OCR stayed unchanged, but the RBNZ explicitly moved into tightening-contingency language under a higher-inflation, weaker-growth shock mix. May MPS is now the decisive next checkpoint.

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 30 Mar 2026 to 3 Apr 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The completed DM week showed the last clean pre-shock hard-data vintage colliding with the first meaningful conflict-era survey layer, and the region resolved into weaker growth quality, limited labour evidence, mixed inflation pressure, tighter effective financial conditions and higher policy constraint. The UK remained the clearest stagflation bind as stagnant growth met worsening cost and terms-of-trade pressure; Japan stayed bifurcated, with resilient large firms, labour and services preserving BoJ optionality but retail, housing and SME capex exposing a softer domestic underbelly; Switzerland delivered the sharpest internal contradiction via collapsing KOF, rebounding PMI and still-low CPI; Sweden split between weak household demand and strong manufacturing; Australia’s headline strength in approvals and trade was composition-distorted while credit stayed firm; and Canada held a relative Q1 floor but entered Q2 with softer industrial and trade-quality momentum. The dominant regime message was not clean continuation of February momentum, but transition away from the pre-war baseline into a more fragile and more policy-complicated DM mix.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
The week’s dominant growth signal was deterioration in breadth and quality rather than outright regional collapse. Japan’s retail sales, housing starts and February IP weakened, Switzerland’s KOF barometer fell sharply below 100, the UK’s Q4 bundle confirmed stagnation, Sweden’s retail sales softened, and Canada’s manufacturing PMI fell to stagnation. Positive offsets existed — Japan services and Tankan large-firm sentiment held up, Sweden manufacturing stayed strong, Swiss PMI rebounded and Canada GDP beat flat consensus — but too many of those supports were either backward-looking, composition-distorted or in direct tension with weaker forward indicators.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
The labour signal was limited and not region-defining. Japan’s unemployment rate improved to 2.6% and the jobs/applicants ratio rose to 1.19, which kept the tight-labour normalization story intact there, but the week did not deliver broad cross-DM labour evidence confirming either a clean deterioration or a fresh tightening impulse. The practical read is stabilisation rather than decisive change.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Inflation resolved into a mixed but less comfortable-quality picture. Tokyo CPI softened and Swiss CPI remained very low, removing immediate tightening urgency for the BoJ and SNB, yet the survey layer in Japan and especially the UK showed a worse cost backdrop: UK manufacturing reported the sharpest input-cost jump since 1992 and longer delivery delays, while Japan PMIs flagged rising input costs and weaker confidence. The region therefore did not produce a broad hard-data inflation reacceleration, but it did show worsening supply-shock inflation risk.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Financial conditions were effectively tighter even where backward-looking credit or housing data still looked firm. The UK’s February BoE credit and mortgage data and March Nationwide prices showed pre-shock resilience, but both were explicitly framed against a now-harsher financing backdrop; Japan’s housing starts deteriorated; and Australia’s still-firm credit growth signalled that transmission remained too loose to relieve policy pressure rather than a supportive easing. The usable macro message was that February resilience did not negate tighter Q2 financing conditions.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
Policy constraint rose because the week increased two-sided difficulty without delivering clean relief on either growth or inflation. The BoE remained in the sharpest bind as stagnant activity met worsening cost pressure; the BoJ retained April optionality thanks to Tankan, labour and services resilience, but softer domestic demand and SME stress argued against aggressive interpretation; the SNB still faced low inflation and mixed growth signals, pointing to hold; the RBA stayed toward the restrictive end of the DM distribution because credit was firm and activity headlines were unreliable; and the BoC gained no clean growth acceleration to offset external and industrial softness.

THURSDAY 2 APR 2026

Japan — Monetary Base YY (Mar)
Release Date: 2 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 01:50

Reuters Data

Monetary Base YY: -11.56% | Prior: -10.58%

Professional Market Commentary

The official BoJ March monetary-base release showed the y/y decline deepening further. There was no meaningful institutional debate around the series itself; it was treated as consistent with ongoing balance-sheet normalization.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was a continuation signal, not a new macro event. The deeper decline reinforced that the BoJ’s balance-sheet stance remained consistent with gradual normalization. It had much less immediate policy information content than Tankan or CPI, but it did fit the broader tightening-bias architecture.
State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM live sequence had Japan on a cautious normalization path. Monetary base contraction remained fully consistent with that baseline.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Low standalone trading value. It matters only as confirming background for the broader BoJ normalization story rather than as a timing trigger.

Australia — Balance on Goods / Goods/Services Imports / Goods/Services Exports (Feb)
Release Date: 2 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 02:30

Reuters Data

Balance on Goods: 5,686M | Prior: 2,631M | Revised: 2,258M | Poll: 2,850M | Surprise: 2,836.0M
Goods/Services Imports: -3.2% | Prior: 0.8% | Revised: 1.1%
Goods/Services Exports: 4.9% | Prior: -0.9% | Revised: -1.6%

Professional Market Commentary

The official ABS release effectively neutralised the headline itself. It said exports rose 4.9% and imports fell 3.2%, with both moves driven by non-monetary gold, and explicitly stated that the February reference period had not yet been impacted by the Middle East conflict; ABS expected those effects to appear from March onward.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was a classic optical-strength release. The headline surplus looked very strong, but the composition showed a safe-haven gold swing doing most of the work, while broader non-rural exports were softer and the conflict was not yet in the data. The only reliable macro read was that ex-gold underlying conditions were much less impressive than the top-line number suggested.
State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM live sequence had Australia firm on domestic transmission but not obviously enjoying a broad external boom. The February trade headline did not overturn that assessment.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The RBA should look through this release. March trade data, not February, is the first proper conflict-era test.

Switzerland — CPI MM / YY (Mar)
Release Date: 2 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 08:30

Reuters Data

CPI MM: 0.2% | Prior: 0.6% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: -0.30%
CPI YY: 0.3% | Prior: 0.1% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: -0.20%

Professional Market Commentary

Reuters framed the release as a modest one-year high driven by fuel, but still below expectations and far too low to create any immediate SNB tightening pressure. UBS’ public comment in the Reuters piece also argued the rise was too modest to trigger rate-hike thinking.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was exactly the kind of print the SNB framework can absorb. Headline inflation rose, but core pressure remained contained and the increase was still tiny by European standards. The broader Swiss problem therefore remained what it was before the release: not overheating, but how to manage weak medium-term inflation with growth signals turning mixed and CHF strength still relevant.
State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file:
There was no prior Swiss CPI block in the live sequence. Within the week, CPI did not resolve the KOF/PMI growth contradiction; it simply removed any near-term inflation urgency.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

June SNB hold remained the base case. CHF and intervention dynamics still mattered more than this CPI print for near-term policy mapping.

Canada — Trade Balance C$ (Feb)
Release Date: 2 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:30

Reuters Data

Trade Balance C$: -5.74B | Prior: -3.65B | Revised: -4.18B | Poll: -2.25B | Surprise: -3.490B

Professional Market Commentary

StatCan showed imports rising 8.4% to a record C$72.1 billion, led by a 45.6% surge in metal and non-metallic mineral imports, notably gold, while exports rose 6.4% to C$66.3 billion. Reuters highlighted the same gold-driven story and stressed that the bilateral surplus with the U.S. narrowed sharply.

Comment — Economist Layer

The headline deficit looked bad, but composition mattered immediately. Gold and precious metals heavily distorted the import side, while motor-vehicle exports recovered. The more durable macro issue was that net trade still looked like a Q1 GDP drag, even if the worst of the headline miss overstated the underlying deterioration.
State transition: deterioration on headline / continuation ex-gold.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM live sequence had Canada stabilising, not accelerating. Trade did not negate that, but it did show that the external sector was not carrying the economy cleanly into Q2.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Near-term BoC mapping was unchanged, but the trade mix mattered for narrative. The politically sensitive part of the release was the sharp narrowing in the U.S. surplus, even if the pure macro signal was softened by gold distortion.

FRIDAY 3 APR 2026

Japan — S&P Global Comp Op Final SA / S&P Global SVC PMI Final SA (Mar)
Release Date: 3 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 02:30

Reuters Data

S&P Global Comp Op Final SA: 53.0 | Prior: 52.5
S&P Global SVC PMI Final SA: 53.4 | Prior: 52.8

Professional Market Commentary

Reuters reported services at 53.4 and composite at 53.0, both still in expansion, but with a weaker quality mix: new business growth slowed, confidence fell to its lowest since September 2020, input costs rose at the fastest pace in nearly a year, and the composite eased from February’s 33-month high.

Comment — Economist Layer

This release kept the Japan services pillar alive, which matters because earlier in the week retail and housing had weakened. But it was not a clean acceleration story. Services remained the stabiliser, while confidence and inflation-quality metrics worsened. In sequence terms, Japan still looked bifurcated: resilient service activity, softer domestic goods demand, and a worsening cost backdrop.
State transition: improvement in services level / continuation in overall regime.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior DM live sequence had Japan’s services side acting as the stabilising counterweight to weaker pockets elsewhere. Final services PMI validated that role again.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This release marginally strengthened the case that the BoJ could still move in April if it wanted to, because the service economy had not rolled over. But the confidence/cost mix still argued for a cautious, optional rather than forceful interpretation.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Hard data still show residual pre-war momentum in places, while the survey layer and forward commentary are already pricing the next regime. Switzerland’s KOF/PMI split is the clearest internal example; Japan’s large-firm Tankan strength versus SME capex stress is the clearest structural example.

Why it matters: It means the region cannot be read off one headline beat or miss. The decisive question is whether April data validate the forward deterioration now visible in surveys and qualitative guidance.

Implication: Treat backward-looking DM strength cautiously. The week’s dominant macro message is regime transition away from the pre-war baseline, not a clean continuation of February momentum.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

Japan — Tankan (1 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This was the dominant release of the week. Large-firm resilience and firmer inflation expectations kept April BoJ optionality alive, but SME capex at -8.1% exposed the first real stress fracture under the surface.
Switzerland — Manufacturing PMI (1 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: Not because the PMI alone is the whole story, but because it directly conflicted with the KOF collapse two days earlier. That makes Switzerland the cleanest “current activity versus forward outlook” tension in the whole DM tape.
United Kingdom — GDP / Current Account / Business Investment (31 Mar 2026)
Why it matters: This block confirmed that the UK entered the shock with stagnant growth and weaker real-income dynamics. The capex revision softened the backward-looking read, but the broader terms-of-trade and policy bind remained the decisive signal.

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 23 Mar 2026 to 27 Mar 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The DM week resolved into a sharper divergence regime, but with the common directional pressure moving toward weaker growth under persistent inflation constraint rather than clean disinflation. The UK delivered the clearest stagflationary deterioration: March PMIs weakened sharply through services, new orders and employment while input costs surged, and February CPI failed to soften enough before the oil shock. Australia also moved away from a simple hawkish-growth story, with flash PMIs slipping into contraction even as trimmed-mean inflation stayed above target. Japan stayed mixed rather than broken: reported CPI softened, but subsidy effects masked firmer underlying inflation, March PMIs still showed expansion, and service PPI confirmed the wage-price channel remained alive.

Norway was the key policy outlier and the highest-information central-bank story in the file. Domestic credit stayed firm, registered unemployment remained stable, and Norges Bank shifted from a cuts framework to an explicit hikes framework despite some softer household-demand fragments and a higher LFS unemployment print. So the final DM regime was not one common cycle but a split between broad growth fragility and still-active inflation pressure, with Norway delivering a hawkish policy escalation while the UK and Australia moved deeper into activity-constrained stagflation territory.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
Forward-looking growth signals deteriorated across the week. Australia’s composite PMI fell to 47.0 with services at 46.6; the UK composite dropped to 51.0 with services missing badly, CBI trades fell to -52, GfK confidence weakened, and February retail sales printed -0.4% m/m after January payback. Japan stayed above 50 in March PMIs but momentum cooled, and chain-store sales slowed sharply. Norway was less weak than the UK or Australia, but February retail sales ex-auto fell -1.1% m/m.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
The labor picture softened but did not collapse uniformly. UK PMIs showed weak employment at 45.5 and softer demand conditions, Norway’s LFS unemployment rose to 4.9% from 4.6%, but registered unemployment held at 2.2% with the seasonally adjusted rate unchanged at 2.1%, which the file treated as the cleaner policy signal. Japan and Australia did not deliver decisive standalone labor breaks in the supplied week, so the DM labor read is softer at the margin but still mixed rather than resolved.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
Underlying inflation pressure remained active across the region. UK core CPI rose to 3.2% and services CPI stayed sticky at 4.3%, with input prices rising and JGM explicitly flagging renewed non-core risk from oil. Australia’s headline CPI eased, but trimmed mean held at 3.3% and was treated as backward-looking pre-shock softness rather than clean policy clearance. Japan’s reported core CPI softened, but core-core stayed at 2.5%, the BOJ’s adjusted gauge later showed 2.2%, and service PPI rose to 2.7%, confirming persistent underlying price pressure. Norway’s MPR explicitly revised inflation and wage assumptions higher.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
The file pointed to tighter effective conditions, but not broad financial rupture. UK company commentary referenced higher borrowing costs and tighter supply conditions, Australia’s PMI commentary also referenced fading confidence under rising costs, and Norway’s hawkish policy-path shift mechanically tightens the rates backdrop. But the week contained little direct hard-data evidence of financial-system stress, so the right score is tighter in felt conditions rather than a clean break.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
Policy constraint intensified materially. Norges Bank delivered the clearest reaction-function shift of the week, moving from a cuts framework to a hikes framework and making a May hike plausible. The BoE’s trade-off worsened as UK growth weakened while core and services inflation stayed sticky and oil risk pointed to higher non-core inflation. The RBA lost clean hiking cover because growth weakened sharply but inflation remained above target, and the BOJ kept its normalisation case alive because underlying inflation held firmer than the headline CPI optics suggested.

MONDAY 23 MAR 2026

Norway — Credit Indicator YY (Feb)
Release Date: 23 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 08:00

Reuters Data

Credit Indicator YY: 4.6% | Prior: 4.4% | Revised: 4.5%

Professional Market Commentary

Official SSB data showed domestic credit growth edging up in February, with household credit broadly steady and corporate credit firmer. There was no meaningful Tier 1 market debate around the release itself, but it sat comfortably alongside Norges Bank’s later message that capacity utilisation remained close to normal and inflation pressures were stronger than previously projected.

Comment — Economist Layer

The signal is mild acceleration rather than a regime break. The important point is that this does not look like a clean cooling-credit backdrop ahead of the Norges meeting. In a DM week where the UK and Australia were already flashing growth stress through surveys, Norway instead entered the week with a still-firm domestic-credit backdrop.
State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file already framed DM as a divergence story rather than one common cycle, with Australia hawkish, Japan mixed, and other pockets softer. February Norway credit fits that logic by adding another sign that not all DM economies were weakening through the same domestic-demand channel.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

Ahead of the Norges decision, this did not force a hike by itself, but it clearly argued against reading Norway through the same soft-growth lens as the UK or New Zealand. Relative policy resilience remained intact.

Australia — S&P Global Mfg PMI Flash / S&P Global Svs PMI Flash / S&P Global Comp PMI Flash (Mar)
Release Date: 23 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 23:00

Reuters Data

S&P Global Mfg PMI Flash: 50.1 | Prior: 51.0
S&P Global Svs PMI Flash: 46.6 | Prior: 52.8
S&P Global Comp PMI Flash: 47.0 | Prior: 52.4

Professional Market Commentary

Official S&P Global framing was unequivocally weak: the private sector fell into contraction for the first time in 18 months, services dropped into contraction for the first time in more than two years, new orders fell, confidence weakened, and input-cost inflation accelerated to the fastest in over three years. Broader S&P analysis also tied March flash PMIs globally to fading growth and rising inflation after the Middle East shock.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the clearest challenge to the earlier “Australia as simple hawkish outlier” story. The hierarchy matters: manufacturing held near 50, but services collapsed below 50 and drove the composite into outright contraction. At the same time, price pressure did not ease enough to give the RBA clean cover. So the release is not a clean dovish turn; it is a stagflationary growth scare.
State transition: sharp deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already flagged Australia as the clearest DM hiker but also showed that the growth side was becoming more fragile after the narrow 5–4 RBA hike and softer leading indicators. The flash PMI materially strengthens that caution.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the RBA, the mapping shifts from “hawkish continuation” to “hawkish with rising activity constraint.” May stays live, but the confidence interval around further tightening is now much wider.

TUESDAY 24 MAR 2026

Japan — CPI, Overall Nationwide / CPI, Core Nationwide YY (Feb)
Release Date: 24 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 00:30

Reuters Data

CPI, Overall Nationwide: 1.3% | Prior: 1.5%
CPI, Core Nationwide YY: 1.6% | Prior: 2.0% | Poll: 1.7% | Surprise: -0.10%

Professional Market Commentary

Official data showed headline CPI at 1.3%, core CPI at 1.6%, and CPI excluding fresh food and energy at 2.5%. Reuters framed the miss as subsidy-distorted rather than demand-clearing disinflation, and later in the week the BOJ published a new adjusted core gauge showing 2.2% for February after stripping out special factors, with adjusted core-core at 2.7%.

Comment — Economist Layer

The important hierarchy is reported inflation versus policy-relevant inflation. On the official government measure, core fell below 2%, which matters for optics and communication. But the broader underlying story remains firmer: core-core stayed at 2.5%, and the BOJ’s own adjusted gauge later showed 2.2%. The cleanest read is therefore not “Japan disinflating below target,” but “policy noise temporarily suppressing the reported measure.”
State transition: false dawn.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file already had Japan in a mixed regime: resilient enough to keep normalisation alive, but not strong enough to argue for an unambiguous hawkish acceleration. February CPI fits that structure. It makes communication harder, but it does not break the tightening case.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This complicates April timing, but not the medium-term normalisation path. The key tactical point is that the BOJ now has an institutionally cleaner measure to defend the underlying inflation case even while the headline softens.

Japan — S&P Global Mfg PMI Flash SA / S&P Global SVC PMI Flash SA / S&P Global Comp Op Flash SA (Mar)
Release Date: 24 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 01:30

Reuters Data

S&P Global Mfg PMI Flash SA: 51.4 | Prior: 53.0
S&P Global SVC PMI Flash SA: 52.8 | Prior: 53.8
S&P Global Comp Op Flash SA: 52.5 | Prior: 53.9

Professional Market Commentary

Official S&P Global commentary described the release as softer expansion rather than reversal: all three indices remained above 50, but momentum cooled, while input-price inflation accelerated sharply. The broader March flash PMI read emphasised fading growth and rising inflation as the dominant regional pattern.

Comment — Economist Layer

The right read is softer momentum plus a cost squeeze. Unlike Australia, Japan did not slip into contraction; unlike the UK, the activity level stayed more comfortably expansionary. But the pace clearly rolled over from February, and the cost side became more relevant than the headline levels. That creates a mild stagflationary texture rather than a clean demand collapse.
State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already shifted Japan away from a clean re-acceleration story: Reuters Tankan services had stabilised, but the trade mix had become less convincing. The March PMI deepens that moderation signal.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the BOJ, this is directionally ambiguous but strategically useful: growth moderation argues for caution, while firmer cost pressure argues against a dovish interpretation of the CPI miss. The normalisation case survives, but timing stays conditional.

United Kingdom — Flash Composite PMI / Flash Manufacturing PMI / Flash Services PMI (Mar)
Release Date: 24 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 10:30

Reuters Data

Flash Composite PMI: 51.0 | Prior: 53.7 | Poll: 52.9 | Surprise: -1.90
Flash Manufacturing PMI: 51.4 | Prior: 51.7 | Poll: 50.1 | Surprise: 1.30
Flash Services PMI: 51.2 | Prior: 53.9 | Poll: 53.0 | Surprise: -1.80

Professional Market Commentary

Official S&P Global and Reuters both treated this as a stagflationary shock print: activity slowed to a six-month low, services missed badly, and manufacturers faced the steepest rise in input costs since 1992. Your JGM note reinforces the cost side: composite input prices rose 6.4 points to 68.2, the highest in three years; manufacturing input prices reached 70.2; supply conditions tightened significantly; new orders fell to 49.7; and employment remained weak at 45.5. The qualitative company commentary in your note is also important: weaker activity was tied to lower confidence, inflation pressure, higher borrowing costs, reduced travel, and more expensive fuel and energy-intensive raw materials.

Comment — Economist Layer

The internal hierarchy is clear. Manufacturing beat consensus, but services missed sharply and services is the more important domestic-demand signal for the UK. JGM helps clarify why the print is more serious than the headline levels alone suggest: this is not only softer output, it is softer output alongside a major cost squeeze, tighter supply conditions, falling new orders, and weak employment. That combination makes the release genuinely stagflationary rather than merely disappointing. The 49.7 new-orders line is especially important because it suggests the growth slowdown is not just contemporaneous but forward-looking.
State transition: sharp deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had only mild UK weakness through the housing channel. This release materially worsens that picture by moving the UK from “soft but still functioning” toward “near-stall with cost shock.”

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This sharply compresses BoE optionality. JGM’s framing is useful here: the policy problem is not just high input costs, but a worsening growth mix under tighter financial conditions. That leaves the BoE facing an ugly configuration where inflation risk is intensifying through energy and raw materials even as demand, hiring, and travel-sensitive activity soften.

United Kingdom — CBI Distributive Trades (Mar)
Release Date: 24 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 12:00

Reuters Data

CBI Distributive Trades: -52 | Prior: -43

Professional Market Commentary

The official CBI release said momentum in retail remained poor, annual sales volumes fell sharply, and there were no signs of an imminent recovery. Reuters added that the March balance was the weakest since April 2020 and that April expectations remained deeply negative.

Comment — Economist Layer

The important point is not the pandemic-era comparison by itself, which is rhetorically strong. The deeper signal is that the UK consumer sector was already fragile before the full lagged energy shock had appeared in hard data. That is what makes this more than a noisy survey miss.
State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had UK housing as rate-sensitive but not broken. The combination of the flash PMI and the CBI release now pulls the broader domestic-demand bucket into a clearly softer regime.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not the single BoE trigger, but it strongly corroborates the PMI message: demand weakness is broadening just as the inflation problem is becoming harder again.

Australia — CPI / Weighted Median / Trimmed Mean (Feb)
Release Date: 25 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 01:30

Reuters Data

CPI MM: 0.00% | Prior: 0.40% | Poll: 0.00% | Surprise: 0.000%
CPI YY: 3.70% | Prior: 3.80% | Poll: 3.80% | Surprise: -0.100%
Weighted Median CPI MM SA: 0.20% | Prior: 0.30%
Weighted Median CPI YY SA: 3.50% | Prior: 3.60%
Trimmed Mean CPI MM SA: 0.20% | Prior: 0.30% | Poll: 0.30% | Surprise: -0.100%
Trimmed Mean CPI YY SA: 3.3% | Prior: 3.4% | Revised: 3.3% | Poll: 3.4% | Surprise: -0.10%

Professional Market Commentary

Official ABS data showed annual CPI easing to 3.7%, with housing the largest contributor at 7.2%, while trimmed mean inflation stayed at 3.3%. ABS also explicitly noted that automotive fuel prices fell in February prior to the Middle East conflict, and Reuters framed the release as slightly soft but backward-looking relative to the incoming energy shock.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is not clean disinflation. Headline eased and the monthly pulse was soft, but the RBA-relevant trimmed mean held at 3.3%, not below it. So the correct read is “rear-view softness, not policy-clearance.” The key issue is timing: February was collected before the full energy shock, which makes this a weak guide to the inflation path the RBA now cares about.
State transition: false dawn.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had Australia as the hawkish outlier, but the 23 March flash PMI had already punctured the clean growth story. February CPI does not undo that warning. It simply says the inflation side remained sticky enough before the shock, while the post-shock growth picture had already weakened.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the RBA, this release is best treated as a pre-shock placeholder. It slightly lowers the immediate inflation baseline, but it does not give clean permission to pause because the trimmed mean is still stuck above target and the next data will carry much more oil pass-through.

CPI YY: 3.0% | Prior: 3.0% | Poll: 3.0% | Surprise: 0.00%
RPI MM: 0.4% | Prior: -0.5% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: -0.10%
RPI YY: 3.6% | Prior: 3.8% | Poll: 3.7% | Surprise: -0.10%
RPIX YY: 3.5% | Prior: 3.7%
CPI Services MM: 0.5% | Prior: -0.3% | Poll: 0.4% | Surprise: 0.10%
CPI Services YY: 4.3% | Prior: 4.4% | Poll: 4.3% | Surprise: 0.00%
PPI Input Prices MM NSA: 0.8% | Prior: 0.4% | Revised: 0.3% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: 0.30%
PPI Input Prices YY NSA: 0.5% | Prior: -0.2% | Revised: -0.4% | Poll: 0.4% | Surprise: 0.10%
PPI Output Prices MM NSA: -0.5% | Prior: 0.0% | Poll: 0.2% | Surprise: -0.70%
PPI Output Prices YY NSA: 1.7% | Prior: 2.5% | Poll: 2.6% | Surprise: -0.90%
PPI Core Output MM NSA: -0.8% | Prior: 0.2%
PPI Core Output YY NSA: 1.9% | Prior: 2.9%

Professional Market Commentary

Official ONS data showed headline CPI unchanged at 3.0%, core CPI rising to 3.2%, and CPI services easing only marginally to 4.3%. ONS highlighted clothing as the main upward force and motor fuels as the main offset, while Reuters stressed that the petrol data still pre-dated the Middle East shock. Your JGM note captures the market takeaway well: mostly inline at the headline level, but core slightly firmer than expected. It also correctly shifts attention to what comes next, namely that non-core inflation is likely to re-accelerate with oil.

Comment — Economist Layer

The signal is stalling disinflation, not renewed progress. Headline was flat, but core ticked up and services remained sticky at 4.3%, still too high for easy BoE comfort. The PPI mix matters too: input prices rose while output prices softened sharply, which points to margin squeeze rather than clean pipeline disinflation. JGM adds an important forward layer: the real issue is not February by itself, but that February did not soften enough before the oil shock. That means the UK enters the next phase with core already slightly ahead and a clear risk that non-core inflation rises as energy feeds through.
State transition: stabilisation with upside risk.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already shifted the UK from mild housing softness to a much more difficult post-PMI configuration. February CPI does not repair that. It says the inflation side was already sticky before the March growth shock showed up in surveys.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the BoE, this is a poor combination: inflation did not improve enough before the shock, and the March surveys already showed growth weakening sharply. JGM’s framing is useful here because it pushes the interpretation beyond “headline in line”: the more relevant macro conclusion is that core was slightly firmer and the next move in non-core is likely up with oil. That makes the BoE’s policy trade-off worse, not better. In your note’s framing, inflation can plausibly move back toward roughly 3.2% in Q3, with materially worse outcomes in adverse oil scenarios.

For the BOJ, this is one of the strongest internal arguments against reading February CPI as a dovish pivot. Underlying service inflation still supports gradual normalisation.

Norway — Labour Force Survey SA (Feb)

Professional Market Commentary

Official SSB updated the Labour Force Survey on 26 March, while Norges Bank’s MPR simultaneously said unemployment had been slightly lower than projected in December. The 30bp jump matters, but it should not be over-read in isolation. Norway has two labour-market lenses, and the LFS is more volatile than the registered series. So the correct interpretation is that labour-market tightness has softened at the margin, but not enough to overturn Norges Bank’s broader “near-normal capacity” story.

Norway — Key Policy Rate (27 Mar effective)
Release Date: 26 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 10:00

Reuters Data

Key Policy Rate: 4.00% | Prior: 4.00% | Poll: 4.00% | Surprise: 0.000%

Professional Market Commentary

Official Norges Bank communication was the clearest DM central-bank shift of the week. The Committee held at 4.0%, said it would likely be appropriate to raise the policy rate at one of the forthcoming meetings, and stated that the revised rate path now points to 4.25%–4.5% by end-2026. The March MPR explicitly cited higher inflation, stronger wage growth, and greater Middle East-related uncertainty. Your JGM note sharpens the interpretation: the communicated path moved from roughly 1–2 cuts in December to 1–2 hikes now; the model revision was driven by higher inflation pressure and higher ULC; some of that was only partly offset further out by a weaker output gap and FX appreciation.

Comment — Economist Layer

The hold itself was not the news. The regime flip was. JGM helps clarify that this was not only a mechanical forecast update but a reaction-function shift. The inflation assumptions were revised up materially, core inflation was marked higher, wage growth assumptions were revised higher, and the Committee’s judgment appears notably more hawkish than a narrow reading of the macro fundamentals alone would have implied. That matters because it suggests Norges was not merely passively following the model; it was actively leaning against upside inflation risk.
State transition: escalation.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file was built before this shift and therefore still sat in a more standard “divergent but recognisable DM cycles” framework. The Norges decision materially sharpens that divergence by creating an explicit Norway-specific hawkish pivot inside a week when the UK and Australia were flashing growth damage.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the single clearest policy-regime signal in the file. JGM’s final inference is important: with inflation assumptions revised up and the wage backdrop potentially more hawkish than before, a May hike becomes plausible rather than merely theoretical. Norway is no longer a “hold and see” DM central bank in market terms; it has become a “hold now, hike later, possibly soon” central bank unless incoming data break sharply lower.

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 16 Mar 2026 to 20 Mar 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

DM macro did not converge to a single regime this week; it split along policy and demand lines. Australia remained the clear inflation/capacity outlier with a second consecutive hike, but the leading index and labour-quality mix softened; the UK shifted decisively into hawkish-hold territory as labour stayed firmer than feared and the MPC suspended the live cut debate; and Japan delivered a tactical hold that preserved medium-term normalization pressure. By contrast, Canada and New Zealand continued to accumulate softer domestic-demand evidence, with Canada printing a cleaner pre-shock disinflation baseline and New Zealand showing weak-quality growth plus still-fragile external repair. Sweden and Switzerland stayed in cautious-hold mode rather than regime change, supported by anchored expectations and FX/energy management.

The weekly macro message is therefore not broad DM reflation or broad DM slowdown, but widening relative-value dispersion under a common oil-shock overlay. Growth signals were mixed to softer outside Japan’s capex pocket and parts of UK labour; labour loosened only gradually where released; inflation pressure was not uniformly re-accelerating, but policy sensitivity to energy persistence rose sharply. The resulting regime was tighter policy constraint in Australia/UK/Japan, softer-growth optionality in Canada/New Zealand, and watchful holds in Sweden/Switzerland.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Canada housing starts rebounded only modestly and still looked flat on the six-month trend; January Canadian retail sales rose but missed consensus; New Zealand Q4 GDP printed only 0.2% q/q with weak expenditure GDP; Australia’s leading index softened again; and UK CBI orders stayed deeply negative. Japan machinery orders and stronger imports prevented a cleaner DM-wide downturn call, but the broad weekly mix was still mixed-to-soft rather than improving decisively.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Australia added 48.9k jobs, but full-time employment fell, part-time rose sharply, and hours worked declined. In the UK, unemployment held at 5.2%, employment beat strongly, payrolls improved, and wage growth slowed further to 3.8%. That combination points to labour markets that are not breaking, but are no longer uniformly tight in quality terms. The week did not resolve the labour signal cleanly.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Canada delivered the cleanest disinflation evidence in the region: CPI fell to 1.8% y/y, core measures moved lower together, and February PPI decelerated sharply. Sweden’s five-year inflation expectations stayed anchored around target. But Australia’s RBA still hiked on domestic inflation/capacity grounds, the BoE explicitly elevated second-round inflation risk, and the BoJ retained its underlying-inflation normalization bias. The inflation backdrop improved in parts of DM, but policy concern about persistence still rose elsewhere.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
UK housing remained clearly rate-sensitive as March prices rose seasonally but supply stayed high, time-to-sale lengthened, and mortgage rates moved up. Australia hiked again to 4.10%, keeping restrictive settings in place even as forward growth softened. Switzerland stayed at 0% but increased its willingness to intervene in FX, showing that financial tightening risk was being managed through the currency channel rather than eased away. The weekly signal was still one of restrictive or tightening financial conditions, not relaxation.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The RBA delivered a second consecutive hike with a 5–4 split, the BoE shifted from a 5–4 cut-biased split to a 9–0 hold, and the BoJ held but with an explicit 1.0% dissenter and no retreat from normalization. The BoC held with a softer domestic baseline, but the oil shock complicated the forward cut path; Sweden and Switzerland also stayed on hold under energy/geopolitical uncertainty. Across the region, central banks remained constrained by inflation persistence or shock-management risk, so policy flexibility narrowed rather than broadened.
