MONDAY 16 MAR 2026

United Kingdom — House Price Rightmove YY (Mar)
Release Date: 16 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 01:01 UK

Reuters Data

House Price Rightmove YY: -0.2% | Prior: 0.0%
House Price Rightmove MM: 0.8% | Prior: 0.0%

Professional Market Commentary

Official Rightmove framing was “steady rather than strong”: March delivered a normal spring asking-price lift, but supply remained at an 11-year high for this point in the year, average time-to-sale was the longest for this stage since 2013, and the average two-year fixed mortgage rate had risen to 4.51% from 4.24% the prior week. Readable housing commentary broadly kept that same emphasis: the market had not broken, but it remained highly price-sensitive and supply-heavy rather than genuinely strong.

Comment — Economist Layer

The y/y move to -0.2% is a mild deterioration from February’s flat annual print, but the more important signal is the internal mix. A +0.8% m/m rise in March is a typical spring seasonal bounce, not renewed pricing power. The problem is that this seasonal lift is occurring alongside excess supply, slower time-to-sale, and renewed mortgage-rate pressure. That combination says sellers are recovering seasonality, not regaining control of pricing. In macro terms, the Rightmove series is behaving like a housing market with too much stock and only limited ability to absorb tighter financing conditions.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had UK housing as subdued rather than collapsing, with Nationwide and Halifax still showing modest resilience while construction PMI remained clearly weak. March Rightmove fits that sequence: the monthly bounce returned, but the annual rate slipped negative, so the housing bucket moved from stall to mild deterioration rather than from stall to recovery.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not a BoE-moving print on its own, but it keeps the housing channel on the “still rate-sensitive” side of the UK story. If the mortgage repricing seen in Rightmove’s tracker persists, housing remains a drag on any clean consumer re-acceleration narrative. Reaction-function relevance is therefore second order: limited direct policy value by itself, but mildly consistent with gradualism rather than any aggressive extrapolation from better near-term activity prints elsewhere.

BoC Core YY: 2.3% | Prior: 2.6%
BoC Core MM: 0.4% | Prior: 0.2%
CPI Median: 2.3% | Prior: 2.5% | Poll: 2.4% | Surprise: -0.10%

Professional Market Commentary

Official and institutional commentary treated the report as a clean but backward-looking disinflation print. Statistics Canada highlighted the GST/HST base effect; the seasonally adjusted monthly pulse was much softer than the raw monthly headline; and the dominant economist framing was that February gives the BoC a benign pre-shock baseline, while March becomes the harder test once the final tax-base effect and oil pass-through overlap.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was not just a headline base-effect story. That is a materially cleaner underlying inflation picture than the raw headline alone implies. Sticky pockets remain, but the right read is: base effects helped the headline, and underlying inflation also genuinely cooled before the energy shock hit.

State transition: improvement.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had Canadian inflation already on a gradual disinflation path, with January core measures softer and the broader Canada macro sequence leaning dovish alongside stalling GDP and labour-market cracks. February extends and broadens that trend: headline moved below 2%, and the preferred core measures all stepped lower together. Relative to the prior sequence, this is a cleaner pre-shock improvement in the inflation baseline.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the 18 March BoC decision, this supports hold with a dovish lean rather than any hike debate. Policymakers can more plausibly look through a temporary oil-led headline rebound because the pre-shock core backdrop has softened and labour slack has increased. The next real verification test is March CPI: that print will combine the final tax base effect with the first fuller oil-shock pass-through.

TUESDAY 17 MAR 2026

Australia — RBA Cash Rate (Mar)
Release Date: 17 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 04:30

Reuters Data

RBA Cash Rate: 4.10% | Prior: 3.85% | Poll: 4.10% | Surprise: 0.000%

Professional Market Commentary

Official RBA communication made clear that the hike was not just an oil-shock reaction. The Board raised the cash rate 25bp to 4.10%, warned of a material risk that inflation stays above target for longer, and recorded a very tight 5–4 split. Governor Bullock’s message was explicit: higher fuel prices add to inflation, but they were not the reason for the decision; inflation was already too high because demand was outstripping supply.

Comment — Economist Layer

The key signal is not the 25bp move itself, which was priced; it is the combination of second consecutive tightening, explicit domestic-demand rationale, and a narrow 5–4 vote. That mix says Australia remains the DM outlier still leaning into inflation control, but the split also says the cycle is closer to its limit than a unanimous hawkish board would imply. The official statement is compositionally important: business investment outperformed, consumption underperformed, labour underutilisation stayed low, and housing had been strong over the prior year. This is a capacity-pressure hike, not an externally forced panic move.

State transition: escalation.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file already had Australia as the only clear DM hiker, with February’s move to 3.85% framed as the start of a live tightening cycle. March confirms that trajectory, but with an important change in texture: February was recorded as unanimous in the prior file, whereas March is explicitly 5–4. So the regime is still hawkish continuation, but with visibly rising internal resistance.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This strengthens the Australia-versus-rest-of-DM divergence trade. Versus peers that are holding or debating cuts, the RBA is still tightening on domestic inflation grounds. But the 5–4 split matters for terminal-rate mapping: May is live, not locked. The hawkish signal is real; confidence around a long sequence of follow-on hikes should be lower than the headline action alone suggests.

The prior DM live file had the January leading index already negative. February extends that sequence. So the forward-growth signal has now weakened for two consecutive months while the RBA has hiked twice.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the main internal check on the RBA hawkish story. Relative rates still support AUD near term, but the over-tightening risk should now be monitored more actively into May. If more labour and spending indicators soften, the case for a third hike becomes materially harder.

Canada — BoC Rate Decision (Mar)
Release Date: 18 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 14:45 ET

Reuters Data

BoC Rate Decision: 2.25% | Prior: 2.25% | Poll: 2.25% | Surprise: 0.000%

Professional Market Commentary

The hold was fully expected, but the statement and press conference were richer than the action. The BoC held at 2.25%, said the economy remains in excess supply, flagged the labour market as soft, and noted that CPI excluding taxes and core measures are all now close to 2%. At the same time, it explicitly described the war in Iran as a new layer of uncertainty and said it would look through the immediate inflation impact, while not allowing high energy prices to broaden into persistent inflation.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a genuine policy fork, not a simple “hold and done” message. The domestic baseline is soft: growth has weakened, unemployment has risen, and core inflation has come down materially. But the oil shock has created an upside inflation risk just as growth is weakening. That is textbook stagflation-style policy tension. The important point is that the BoC did not validate a renewed hiking bias; it kept the burden of proof on persistence in inflation spillovers, not on the first-round energy move.

State transition: no material change in action, regime fork in forward path.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had January’s BoC hold framed as a tactical pause inside an easing-biased regime. Since then, the live sequence has added soft growth, weak labour, softer housing starts, and a cleaner February CPI baseline. March’s hold therefore continues the pause, but with more two-sided uncertainty because the oil shock now interrupts what had become a progressively more dovish domestic data path.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The BoC is still much closer to a future cut debate than to a hike debate, but timing has become less clean. Near term, the Bank has room to wait because core inflation is close to target and the economy has slack. The decisive verification test is March CPI: if oil lifts headline but core spillovers stay contained, the cut path reopens later in 2026.

New Zealand — GDP Prod Based QQ / YY / Ann Avg / GDP Exp Based QQ (Q4)
Release Date: 18 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 22:45

Reuters Data

GDP Prod Based QQ, SA: 0.2% | Prior: 1.1% | Poll: 0.4% | Surprise: -0.20%
GDP Prod Based YY, SA: 1.3% | Prior: 1.3% | Poll: 1.7% | Surprise: -0.40%
GDP Prod Based, Ann Avg: 0.2% | Prior: -0.5% | Poll: 0.3%
GDP Exp Based QQ, SA: 0.1% | Prior: 1.3% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: -0.40%

Professional Market Commentary

Official and Reuters reporting point in the same direction: GDP rose only 0.2% q/q in Q4, below both market and RBNZ expectations, while expenditure GDP rose just 0.1%. Stats NZ’s release and news summary emphasised that GDP per capita was flat and that annual growth for the December 2025 year was only 0.2%.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is technically positive growth but weak-quality growth. The production measure rose 0.2%, yet the expenditure measure rose only 0.1%, which is the more worrying line. Official reporting also showed a narrow mix rather than broad demand strength, with construction weak and tourism-linked services doing more of the lifting. That is exactly why the GDP result matters for the RBNZ: it implies more spare capacity than the Bank had likely assumed entering the oil shock.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already shown a mixed NZ picture: better dairy and terms of trade, but only moderate retail-volume growth and still-soft household demand. Q4 GDP resolves that tension in the softer direction. Export-price support helped, but the broader domestic recovery remained too narrow to deliver a convincing growth handoff into year-end.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is modestly dovish for the RBNZ, even with oil complicating the near-term inflation path. A weaker output backdrop gives the Bank more room to absorb first-round energy inflation without needing to tighten. In FX terms, the combination of weak GDP, a wider current-account deficit, and Australia’s tighter policy path keeps the relative-pressure story on NZD versus AUD intact.

THURSDAY 19 MAR 2026

Japan — JP BOJ Rate Decision (Mar)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: JST morning

Reuters Data

JP BOJ Rate Decision: 0.75% | Prior: 0.75% | Poll: 0.75% | Surprise: 0.000%

Professional Market Commentary

The official BoJ statement delivered a clear hawkish hold rather than a dovish pause. The Policy Board kept the overnight call-rate target at around 0.75% by an 8–1 vote, stated that Japan’s economy “has recovered moderately, although some weakness has been seen in part,” and left the medium-term underlying-inflation view intact. Crucially, the statement also recorded Hajime Takata’s proposal to raise the rate to around 1.0%, which was defeated by majority vote.

Comment — Economist Layer

The hold was tactical, not strategic. The key information was the vote structure and the retained normalization bias. An 8–1 hold with an explicit 1.0% dissenter means the center of gravity on the Board is still moving in a hawkish direction even if the Bank chose to wait through a new oil-risk shock. The official text also still projects underlying CPI inflation to rise gradually and be generally consistent with target later in the January projection window. That is not a pivot away from hikes.

State transition: no material change in action / escalating normalization pressure in forward guidance.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already built a medium-term BoJ normalization story around firm services prices, solid capex, and uneven but positive activity. March’s decision preserves that structure. What changed is the tactical overlay: the Bank paused at 0.75%, but the emergence of an explicit 1.0% proposal inside the Board makes the next-step debate more live than before.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is a hawkish hold for JPY medium-term mapping, even if the immediate market read can still be JPY-soft because no hike was delivered. The binding issue is timing, not direction. April is now a credible live meeting if wage, FX, and inflation signals remain firm.

Japan — Machinery Orders MM / YY (Jan)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 00:50 JST

Reuters Data

Machinery Orders MM: -5.5% | Prior: 19.1% | Revised: 16.1% | Poll: -9.6% | Surprise: 4.10%
Machinery Orders YY: 13.7% | Prior: 16.8% | Poll: 10.5% | Surprise: 3.20%

Professional Market Commentary

Official and readable commentary treated January as a payback from December’s very strong spike rather than a capex breakdown. The Cabinet Office kept its basic assessment that machinery orders are showing “signs of picking up,” while Japan Times reporting noted that manufacturers’ orders were dragged lower by the unwind of large one-off orders in December.

Comment — Economist Layer

The m/m fall is less important than the y/y signal and the context. December had been exceptionally strong and was revised down only modestly; January’s -5.5% therefore looks like a partial normalization, not a collapse. The y/y pace at 13.7% and the retained official assessment both support the idea that capex momentum is still positive. The Iran shock now matters more as a forward risk to boardroom confidence than the backward-looking January decline itself.

State transition: improvement versus forecast / continuation in the underlying capex trend.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had December machinery orders at +19.1% m/m and +16.8% y/y and already treated that as a constructive capex signal. January cools the monthly pace but preserves the broader message: business investment has not rolled over.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is additive to the BoJ’s medium-term normalization story because it supports the business-investment side of the cycle. It does not force a near-term hike, but it makes it easier for the BoJ to keep a tightening bias while waiting for more clarity on the external shock.

Australia — Employment / Participation Rate / Unemployment Rate (Feb)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 01:30 AEDT

Reuters Data

Employment: 48.9k | Prior: 17.8k | Revised: 26.1k
Participation Rate: 66.9% | Prior: 66.7% | Poll: 66.7% | Surprise: 0.20%
Unemployment Rate: 4.3% | Prior: 4.1% | Poll: 4.1% | Surprise: 0.20%

Professional Market Commentary

The ABS release was mixed in exactly the way that matters for the RBA. Employment rose by 49,000, but part-time jobs increased by 79,000 while full-time jobs fell by 30,000; hours worked fell 0.2%; and the participation rate rose to 66.9%, pushing the unemployment rate up to 4.3%. The official ABS explanation was that more people remained unemployed this February than in recent Februarys and fewer moved straight from unemployment into work.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is not a simple “strong jobs report.” The headline employment gain is real, but the quality mix softened: full-time fell, part-time surged, and hours worked declined. The unemployment increase is also largely supply-driven via participation rather than a clear demand collapse. That means the report does not invalidate the RBA’s labour-tightness narrative, but it does show that the labour market is no longer uniformly tight across quality metrics.

State transition: mild deterioration in composition against continuation in the headline.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had Australia already diverging hawkishly on inflation, but the 18 March leading index had just added a softer-growth counterpoint. February labour data reinforce that internal tension: hiring continues, yet labour quality and hours are deteriorating at the margin.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

May remains live, but less cleanly than the post-RBA headline might have suggested. Relative-rate support for AUD is still there, yet the full-time/part-time split and weaker hours are exactly the kind of early-softening evidence that can cap the hiking path if it persists.

The UK labour bucket therefore moved from softening to soft-but-stable.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For policy mapping, the key point is that wages continued to cool while activity-style labour indicators were less weak than feared. That combination removed pressure for near-term cuts without giving the MPC enough to justify a hike. It was the ideal pre-BoE “hawkish hold” backdrop.

For Sweden, anchored five-year expectations matter more than short-term energy noise. Switzerland — SNB Policy Rate (Q1)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 09:30

Reuters Data

SNB Policy Rate: 0.00% | Prior: 0.00% | Poll: 0.00% | Surprise: 0.000%

Professional Market Commentary

The SNB left its policy rate unchanged at 0% and explicitly said that, given the conflict in the Middle East, its willingness to intervene in the foreign-exchange market had increased. That is the key signal: Switzerland stayed at the lower bound and leaned on FX optionality rather than rates as the first response tool.

Comment — Economist Layer

At 0%, the SNB is already operating with little conventional-rate room. So the decision itself was not the story. The official emphasis on greater willingness to intervene tells you the Bank sees CHF appreciation risk as the main tightening channel from the geopolitical shock. That is very different from the UK, Japan, or Australia, where the policy issue is inflation persistence and whether more restriction may be needed.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had Switzerland already at the low end of the DM policy-rate spectrum and more sensitive to FX conditions than to domestic overheating. March preserves exactly that configuration.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This keeps CHF as a policy-sensitive safe haven. The immediate reaction function is not “SNB hikes” but “SNB watches the franc and reserves the right to lean against appreciation.” That matters more for CHF crosses than for Swiss domestic-cycle trades.

The Riksbank left the policy rate unchanged at 1.75% and explicitly said that inflationary pressures are weaker, but energy prices have risen tangibly, and that the war in the Middle East is creating uncertainty even though conditions for continued recovery are fundamentally favourable. Sweden is not being forced into the same hawkish reclassification as the UK. The official language shows a central bank that sees weaker underlying inflation pressure and better recovery conditions, but is temporarily constrained by energy uncertainty. That is meaningfully different from the BoE’s worry about second-round effects or the RBA’s domestic-capacity problem.

United Kingdom — BOE Bank Rate / MPC Votes (Mar)
Release Date: 19 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:00

Reuters Data

BOE MPC Vote Hike: 0 | Prior: 0 | Poll: 0 | Surprise: 0.0

Professional Market Commentary

This was the clearest hawkish surprise of the DM week. The BoE summary says the MPC voted unanimously to keep Bank Rate at 3.75%, and the official text says the MPC is alert to the increased risk of domestic inflationary pressures through second-round wage and price-setting effects the longer higher energy prices persist. The official minutes also state that a larger or more protracted shock would require a more restrictive policy stance.

Comment — Economist Layer

The 9–0 vote is the regime shift. In February the MPC split 5–4 with four members voting to cut; in March all four of those members moved back to hold. That is not a marginal change. The prior DM live file had February’s BoE as a hold with a live cut debate. March changes that decisively. The UK policy bucket has moved from “timing of cuts” to “how long to hold, and under what circumstances hike-risk becomes live.”

HF Take — Strategist Layer

GBP and the front end should treat this as a material higher-for-longer signal. The next step is not necessarily a hike, but the cut path has been suspended unless oil shock persistence fades quickly and wage disinflation continues. Relative to Canada, this sharply widens the policy divergence.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: The same energy shock pushed the UK and Japan toward hawkish holds and left Australia still hiking, while Canada and New Zealand continued to accumulate soft-demand evidence that keeps future cut optionality alive.
Why it matters: There is no single DM policy response to the shock.
Implication: Relative-value trades across DM central banks and FX pairs matter more than any aggregate “DM macro” narrative this week.

United Kingdom — BOE Bank Rate / MPC Votes (19 Mar 2026)
Why it matters: The 9–0 hold was the sharpest policy surprise of the week. It marked a full regime change from February’s 5–4 cut-biased split and reclassified the UK from “when to cut” to “how long to hold under inflation risk.”
Japan — JP BOJ Rate Decision (19 Mar 2026)

<<DM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 9 Mar 2026 to 13 Mar 2026

A. DM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

DM growth weakened over the week, but with clear cross-country dispersion rather than a uniform downturn. Canada and the UK delivered the strongest real-economy softness, with trade, wholesale, manufacturing sales, labour, GDP, services, and housing-sensitive channels all pointing to weak pre-energy-shock domestic demand. Sweden’s January hard-data batch also broke the prior recovery hope, even as inflation stayed cleanly below target and labour remained soft rather than collapsing. Against that, Japan remained the relative firm spot: Q4 GDP and capex were revised up credibly, external balances improved modestly, and pipeline inflation softened, though household demand still failed to validate a clean consumption handoff. Norway sat between those poles, with inflation cooling but not enough to reopen an easy easing path, while New Zealand manufacturing stayed solid without changing the broader mixed demand picture.

The final weekly regime is therefore differentiated prolonged hold, not DM convergence. Sweden looks like the cleanest disinflation case, Norway remains inflation-constrained, the BoE faces a stagflation-style bind, the BoC is increasingly constrained by weakening demand, and the BoJ retains gradual normalization optionality but not urgency. The shared oil/inflation overlay matters, but the more important point is that DM central banks are entering it from very different domestic-growth starting points.

B. DM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: -
Supporting evidence:
The weekly growth signal deteriorated. Canada printed a large trade miss, weaker wholesale sales, sharply lower manufacturing sales, and a very weak labour report, while the UK delivered flat monthly GDP, stalled services, soft industrial output, and a weaker housing survey. Sweden’s January GDP, production, industrial output, and orders also rolled over materially. The main offset was Japan, where Q4 GDP and capex were revised up in a compositionally credible way, with New Zealand PMI also staying expansionary, but those positives were not enough to overturn the broader DM softness.

Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
The labour signal worsened, led by Canada and reinforced by Sweden. Canada lost 83.9k jobs, unemployment rose to 6.7%, full-time and private-sector employment weakened, and youth unemployment rose sharply, making this a clear negative demand-sensitive labour print. Sweden’s unemployment rate rose to 8.8% on the NSA comparison, keeping labour conditions soft even if adjusted trends were less alarming. No strong offsetting labour-positive release elsewhere in the file was large enough to neutralize that deterioration.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Inflation was mixed rather than directionally resolved. Sweden confirmed a clean disinflation profile with CPIF at 1.7% y/y and CPIF ex energy at 1.4%, while Japan’s CGPI softened and Norway’s CPI/core CPI cooled from January’s shock. But Norway core inflation at 3.0% remained too high for an easy dovish read, and UK commentary increasingly sat under an oil-linked inflation-risk overlay. The net result is lower immediate inflation panic in parts of DM, but not a clean region-wide easing signal.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week pointed to tighter rate-sensitive conditions in selected channels, but not to a single DM-wide financial-conditions regime shift. UK housing sentiment deteriorated as mortgage-rate expectations and geopolitical/oil risks fed through buyer enquiries and sales expectations, while Canada’s and the UK’s weak demand data argue against tightening through domestic overheating. Sweden’s weak activity and low inflation preserve easier policy optionality, but Norway’s still-elevated core inflation limits that effect. Conditions therefore looked uneven and country-specific rather than uniformly tightening or easing.

Policy Constraint: ~
Supporting evidence:
The common policy outcome is prolonged hold, but for different reasons across DM. The BoC is increasingly constrained by demand weakness after back-to-back soft Canadian activity and labour releases. The BoE faces a classic bind: flat domestic demand and weak housing, but a higher oil/inflation overlay. The Riksbank still has cut optionality because inflation is below target and labour remains soft, while Norges Bank remains more constrained by still-high core inflation. Japan’s stronger capex/growth pulse keeps BoJ normalization optionality alive, but patchy household demand and softer pipeline inflation prevent urgency.

C. FULL RELEASE ARCHIVE

JAPAN — Current Account / Trade Balance (Jan)

Release Date: 9 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 00:50

Reuters Data

Current Account NSA: ¥941.6B | Prior: ¥728.8B
Trade Balance: -¥600.4B | Prior: ¥134.9B

Professional Market Commentary

Public institutional debate was thin, so the mini tape rests mainly on the official balance-of-payments release. The main official emphasis was that exports rose 20.3% y/y and imports fell 7.7% y/y, narrowing the goods deficit, but the broader goods-and-services balance remained negative while primary income stayed the main surplus engine.

Comment — Economist Layer

The signal is improvement, but not transformation. January moved back to a modest current-account surplus and the goods deficit narrowed materially, which is directionally better than the softer December profile. But the structure still matters more than the headline: Japan’s external cushion remains heavily income-led, not trade-led, so this is not a clean external-demand breakout.

State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already framed Japan’s external position as surplus-positive but structurally income-led rather than a decisive trade-volume story. January fits that template: better than December on the headline, but still not the kind of trade-led shift that would materially alter the BoJ map.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is mildly supportive for JPY stability and for the idea that Japan is not facing an external-balance stress event. But it remains second-order for BoJ pricing. Unless external improvement starts to coincide with firmer consumption and stickier domestic inflation, the release stays supportive rather than catalytic.

JAPAN — Household Spending (Jan)

Release Date: 10 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 00:30

Reuters Data

Household Spending YY, Real: -1.0% | Prior: -2.6%

Professional Market Commentary

The public tape read this as a softer consumption print that tempers the stronger Q4 GDP revision released the same day. Official detail showed real spending still down on the year despite firmer worker income, while market coverage highlighted that the result undercut expectations for a positive y/y rebound.

Comment — Economist Layer

The key point is not collapse but inconsistency. Spending improved relative to December’s deep contraction, yet it still printed negative and failed to validate a clean handoff from wage growth into household demand. That matters because private consumption is the weak flank of the Japan normalization story: capex is firmer, but the household side still looks uneven.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had just seen an upside surprise in November household spending and was using that to argue households were not retrenching as feared. January partly reverses that reassurance. It does not erase the broader domestic-demand stabilization story, but it reintroduces caution around extrapolating consumption strength too aggressively.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For BoJ reaction-function purposes, this print slightly offsets the hawkish read from the GDP/capex revision. The practical mix becomes: capex credible, consumption still patchy. That argues for continued gradualism rather than a clean acceleration in tightening expectations.

JAPAN — GDP Revised / Capex (Q4 2025)

Release Date: 10 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 00:50

Reuters Data

GDP QoQ: 0.3% | Prior: 0.1% | Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: 0.00%
GDP Annualized: 1.3% | Prior: 0.2% | Poll: 1.2% | Surprise: +0.10%
Capex QoQ: 1.3% | Prior: 0.2% | Poll: 1.1% | Surprise: +0.20%

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional commentary focused on the quality of the upward revision rather than the headline alone. Reuters and Cabinet Office detail both pointed to a capex-led upgrade, a firmer private-consumption estimate, and a domestic-demand contribution revised up to 0.3pp, while external demand stayed flat.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a better revision than a purely statistical one. Capex moved from marginal to meaningfully positive, and private consumption was also revised up, which makes the Q4 composition more credible as domestic-demand stabilization rather than inventory or external noise. It is still not a full demand boom, but it clearly improves the quality of Japan’s late-2025 growth pulse.

State transition: reversal.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had read the first Q4 GDP estimate as too soft for a clean BoJ-normalization backdrop, even though MOF capex and machinery-order signals were already improving. This revision validates that earlier capex improvement and upgrades the prior soft-GDP interpretation rather than creating a wholly new growth regime.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This keeps Japan out of the weak-growth bucket inside DM. Relative to Europe and Canada, Japan’s macro mix now looks firmer on growth while still not forcing an immediate hawkish inflation conclusion. That is constructive for Japan’s relative rates/FX story, even if the household side still prevents a full hawkish extrapolation.

SWEDEN — GDP / Production (Jan)

Release Date: 10 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 08:00

Reuters Data

GDP MM: -1.1%
GDP YY: 0.6%
Private Sector Production MM: -1.0%
Industrial Production MM: -5.7%
Industrial Orders MM: -5.5%

Professional Market Commentary

Public institutional commentary was limited, so the mini tape is official-led. Statistics Sweden’s January batch showed a sharp monthly GDP drop, a 1.0% fall in overall private-sector production, a 5.7% fall in industrial production, and a 5.5% drop in industrial orders, with both domestic and export orders down.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a materially weak monthly hard-data set. The magnitude of the industrial-production and orders declines says the January setback was not just a soft GDP indicator print. Services were roughly flat to slightly positive, but the industrial side rolled over hard, so the more optimistic late-February Sweden activity story did not carry into January.

State transition: false dawn.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had shifted Sweden toward a “disinflating without outright collapse” configuration after firmer Q4 GDP, better retail sales, and a strong manufacturing PMI. January weakens that improvement materially. The message now is that Sweden’s recovery remained fragile and still vulnerable to monthly reversals in the industrial complex.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This reopens the growth side of the Riksbank debate. Sweden still has room to stay patient because inflation is low, but the January activity hit makes it harder to argue that growth alone justifies a long hold. SEK-positive growth re-rating is not supported by this release.

NORWAY — CPI / Core CPI (Feb)

Release Date: 10 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 08:00

Reuters Data

CPI YY: 2.7% | Prior: 3.6%
Core CPI (CPI-ATE) YY: 3.0% | Prior: 3.4%
CPI MM: 0.6% | Prior: 0.6%
Core CPI (CPI-ATE) MM: 0.7% | Prior: 0.3%

Professional Market Commentary

The institutional read was that February reversed January’s inflation shock only partially. Public market coverage framed the core print as in line with expectations and enough to keep easing optionality alive, but not enough to restore a clean dovish narrative given CPI-ATE at 3.0% and still above Norges Bank’s target-consistent zone.

Comment — Economist Layer

This was a useful cooling print, but not a benign one. Headline and core both stepped down from January, which removes the immediate “inflation re-acceleration” scare. But core inflation at 3.0% is still too high to imply an easy easing path, especially after January’s upside surprise and with oil-sensitive upside risks sitting in the background.

State transition: stabilization.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had treated January CPI as a first-order reaction-function shock that pushed Norges easing expectations out. February softens that signal, but only into a “still too high, just less alarming” regime. So the state change is toward stabilization, not reversal back to a clearly dovish setup.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This reduces the probability of another immediate NOK-positive hawkish repricing, but it does not restore a rapid-cut story. Norges Bank still looks constrained versus Sweden and more inflation-sticky than markets would like. The practical implication is fewer hawkish tails, not a clean dovish reopening.

JAPAN — Corp Goods Prices (Feb)

Reuters Data

Corp Goods Price MM: -0.1% | Prior: 0.2% | Poll: 0.1% | Surprise: -0.20%
Corp Goods Price YY: 2.0% | Prior: 2.3% | Poll: 2.1% | Surprise: -0.10%

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional commentary framed the release as softer wholesale inflation rather than a regime break. Reuters emphasized that government fuel subsidies helped suppress pipeline inflation in February, while the BoJ release and subsequent commentary warned that the Iran-linked oil shock and weak yen could push import costs back up.

Comment — Economist Layer

The near-term signal is moderation. A negative monthly print and slower y/y pace say goods-pipeline pressure eased again in February. But the inflation quality matters: subsidies helped hold the line, while yen-based import prices accelerated sharply, so the underlying cost-push risk has not disappeared.

State transition: stabilization.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file already had Japan in a “growth better than feared, inflation not forcing urgency” configuration after softer Tokyo/nationwide CPI and better activity data. February CGPI reinforces that balance by leaning softer on wholesale inflation without overturning the firmer capex/growth message.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This modestly lowers the urgency of a near-term hawkish inflation interpretation. The BoJ mix now looks more balanced: firmer domestic growth, but no fresh pipeline-inflation acceleration yet. That keeps June normalization optionality alive without making it automatic.

UNITED KINGDOM — RICS Housing Survey (Feb)

Release Date: 12 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 01:01

Reuters Data

RICS Housing Survey: -12 | Prior: -10 | Poll: -9 | Surprise: -3.0

Professional Market Commentary

The institutional tape was clear: February housing sentiment deteriorated as geopolitical risk and higher oil prices fed back into mortgage-rate expectations. RICS and Reuters both highlighted the collapse in new buyer enquiries to -26 from -15, weaker near-term sales expectations, and pronounced regional divergence with London and the South East under the most pressure.

Comment — Economist Layer

The headline miss understates the softness. Buyer enquiries and near-term price expectations deteriorated more sharply than the top-line survey balance, which means the rate-sensitive part of the housing channel remains fragile. The geopolitical/oil explanation matters, but the deeper vulnerability is structural affordability: the survey still points to a market that can stabilize but not absorb a “higher for longer” mortgage-rate shock very well.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had UK housing in a “stabilising, not recovering” state, with December RICS less weak than feared and Nationwide still modestly positive, but construction PMI had already rolled over sharply. February breaks the mild-stabilisation story and shifts housing back toward a clear policy-sensitive drag.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is dovish on the growth side for the BoE, but the transmission is messy because it runs through oil, inflation expectations, and mortgage rates simultaneously. The practical read is not “cuts soon,” but “housing is again a vulnerable transmission channel if the oil shock delays easing.”

There was no fresh debate in public markets because the final print exactly confirmed the flash estimate. This keeps Riksbank cut optionality alive even after the weak January activity batch. Sweden now looks like the cleanest disinflation case among the major European DM names in this file. The only real complication is imported energy, not domestic price momentum.

CANADA — Trade Balance / Building Permits / Wholesale Trade (Jan)

Release Date: 12 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:30

Reuters Data

Building Permits MM: 4.8% | Prior: 6.8% | Revised: 6.1% | Poll: -1.3% | Surprise: 6.10%
Trade Balance C$: -3.65B | Prior: -1.31B | Revised: -1.30B | Poll: -0.90B | Surprise: -2.750B
Wholesale Trade MM: -1.0% | Prior: 2.0% | Revised: 1.8% | Poll: -0.6% | Surprise: -0.40%

Professional Market Commentary

The institutional tape focused overwhelmingly on trade weakness. Statistics Canada and Reuters highlighted the 4.7% drop in exports, the 21.2% fall in motor-vehicle exports, weaker gold shipments, and the widening non-U.S. deficit, while economists generally argued that strong permits were not enough to offset the broader soft-activity message. Public house views also leaned toward “BoC hold reinforced” because weak trade and wholesale data counterbalanced the oil-driven inflation risk.

Comment — Economist Layer

The dominant signal is deterioration in real-economy momentum. The trade miss was large, export volumes fell, and wholesale sales weakened, which together point to a softer January activity starting point than expected. Building permits were a genuine positive surprise, but they were compositionally non-residential-heavy and far too small to offset the broader external and distribution-side weakness. The auto slump is partly temporary retooling, but the widening non-U.S. deficit and weak export profile argue against dismissing the release as pure noise.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had Canada in a soft-growth but not collapsing regime: Q4 GDP had stalled, inflation had softened, and December trade had narrowed modestly. January reverses that partial stabilization. The message shifts from “weak but manageable” to “growth softness reasserting itself in a more visible way.”

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is an important BoC signal because it weakens the case for any hawkish response to the oil shock. Canada is starting from a softer demand base than the inflation backdrop alone might suggest. Directionally that is CAD-negative and argues for policy patience rather than pre-emptive tightening.

NEW ZEALAND — Manufacturing PMI (Feb)

Release Date: 12 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 22:30

Reuters Data

Manufacturing PMI: 55.0 | Prior: 55.2

Professional Market Commentary

The BusinessNZ/BNZ tape described February as another solid expansion print rather than an acceleration event. The main emphasis was that new orders and production remained clearly above long-run averages, with employment only gradually catching up, and that the PMI was too early to reflect the Middle East shock.

Comment — Economist Layer

The signal is continuity. A slip from 55.2 to 55.0 is negligible at this level and still points to a manufacturing sector expanding at a healthy pace. That matters because it leaves New Zealand looking firmer on factory activity than on household-demand proxies, which had been softer.

State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file already had January PMI at 55.2 and a broader New Zealand mix of improving manufacturing/terms of trade but softer household demand. February confirms that pattern rather than changing it. Manufacturing remains the firmer side of the NZ macro picture.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is mildly supportive for the NZ growth floor, but it is not a regime changer for the RBNZ on its own. The more important implication is relative: manufacturing is not where the weakness is. That keeps NZ in the “mixed but not deteriorating sharply” bucket inside DM.

FRIDAY 13 MAR 2026

UNITED KINGDOM — GDP / Services / Industrial Output / Manufacturing / Trade (Jan)

Release Date: 13 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 08:00

Reuters Data

GDP Est 3M/3M: 0.2% | Prior: 0.1% | Poll: 0.3% | Surprise: -0.10%
GDP Estimate MM: 0.0% | Prior: 0.1% | Poll: 0.2% | Surprise: -0.20%
GDP Estimate YY: 0.8% | Prior: 0.7% | Poll: 0.9% | Surprise: -0.10%
Services MM: 0.0% | Prior: 0.3% | Revised: 0.2% | Poll: 0.2% | Surprise: -0.20%
Services YY: 0.9% | Prior: 0.8% | Poll: 1.0% | Surprise: -0.10%
Industrial Output MM: -0.1% | Prior: -0.9% | Poll: 0.2% | Surprise: -0.30%
Industrial Output YY: 0.4% | Prior: 0.5% | Poll: 0.6% | Surprise: -0.20%
Manufacturing Output MM: 0.1% | Prior: -0.5% | Poll: 0.2% | Surprise: -0.10%
Manufacturing Output YY: 1.3% | Prior: 0.5% | Poll: 1.5% | Surprise: -0.20%
Goods Trade Balance GBP: -14.449B | Prior: -22.724B | Revised: -22.724B | Poll: -22.200B | Surprise: 7.7510B

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional commentary framed the release as a weak activity report with one important offset: monthly GDP flatlined, services stalled, and industrial activity remained soft, but the goods trade balance improved sharply. Public house views emphasized that the activity weakness arrived before the Middle East energy shock fully transmits, which strengthens the stagflation framing into the 19 March BoE meeting. The trade improvement was acknowledged but generally treated as a less durable signal than the flat GDP/services profile. ONS official detail also showed that the three-month growth rate improved to 0.2%, so the release was weak, but not a full collapse print.

Comment — Economist Layer

The activity side was clearly soft, but the release is more mixed than a simple “UK rolled over” headline. Monthly GDP was flat, services showed no growth, and industrial output still fell, which matters because services remains the core UK growth engine. The weak part of services was also identifiable rather than diffuse: employment activities fell 5.7% and were the single largest negative drag on both services and total GDP, while accommodation and food services also fell materially. That supports the scout tape’s point that the monthly growth miss was real, even if some components may be noisy.

The counterweight is that this was not uniformly weak. Manufacturing output was slightly positive m/m, the three-month GDP rate improved from 0.1% to 0.2%, and trade was much better than expected. ONS reported goods exports up £2.0bn in January, imports down £0.3bn, and the three-month total goods-and-services deficit narrowing by £5.1bn to £1.8bn. That does not overturn the weak domestic-demand message, but it does mean the release was softer on internal demand than on external arithmetic.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had UK activity in a fragile stabilization regime: November GDP had beaten, services PMIs were still in expansion, but construction and housing-sensitive channels were already weak. January shifts that regime lower. The deterioration is not absolute because 3M/3M growth improved and trade helped, but the core message is that domestic momentum was already soft before the energy shock.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is still growth-dovish for the BoE, but the release does not restore a March-cut narrative because the inflation shock now sits on top of a flat activity base. The most useful way to frame it is: pre-shock demand was already weak, post-shock inflation risk is higher, and trade improvement is not strong enough to remove the bind. That keeps the BoE in prolonged-hold territory rather than pushing toward either near-term cuts or a true hawkish pivot.

SWEDEN — Unemployment Rate (Feb)

Release Date: 13 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 08:00

Reuters Data

Unemployment Rate: 8.8% | Prior: 8.6%

Professional Market Commentary

There was little standalone institutional debate around this release. Official Statistics Sweden detail showed unemployment at 8.8% in February on a non-seasonally-adjusted basis, but also noted that seasonally adjusted and smoothed data showed employment increasing and unemployment decreasing. So the public reading is best described as a soft labour market in level terms, but not a decisive fresh deterioration signal.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is weak in level, but mixed in interpretation. An 8.8% unemployment rate keeps Sweden’s labour market clearly softer than what would normally fit a firm recovery narrative, and relative to the prior 8.6% Reuters comparison it points to some deterioration. But the official release also matters: Sweden is not describing the labour market as rolling over in adjusted trend terms. That prevents over-reading one monthly NSA print, especially after the stronger year-on-year employment count.

State transition: no material change.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file already had Sweden in a “low inflation, weak labour market, fragile recovery” configuration. February unemployment does not materially change that architecture. It reinforces the labour-softness side of the story, but not enough to create a new regime shift on its own.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This marginally supports the dovish wing of the Riksbank debate when combined with CPIF below target, but it is too mixed to be a clean standalone cut trigger. The better framing is that Sweden still lacks labour-market strength to offset the weak inflation impulse.

CANADA — Employment / Unemployment / Manufacturing Sales / Capacity Utilization (Feb/Q4/Jan)

Release Date: 13 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:30

Reuters Data

Capacity Utilization: 78.5% | Prior: 78.5% | Revised: 78.9%
Employment Change: -83.9k | Prior: -24.8k | Poll: 10.0k | Surprise: -93.90k
Unemployment Rate: 6.7% | Prior: 6.5% | Poll: 6.6% | Surprise: 0.10%
Manufacturing Sales MM: -3.0% | Prior: 0.6% | Revised: 0.4% | Poll: -3.3% | Surprise: 0.30%

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional commentary treated the labour report as the dominant signal by a wide margin. The print was described in public macro coverage as one of the worst non-pandemic employment reports in years, with the key policy implication that a BoC rate hike in response to the oil shock is effectively off the table. Manufacturing sales and capacity utilization reinforced, rather than diluted, that message: manufacturing sales fell sharply in January and industrial capacity utilization slipped to 78.5% in Q4, pointing to softer demand and less capacity pressure than previously thought. Statistics Canada’s official releases also showed the labour weakness was broad enough to matter: employment fell by 84,000, the unemployment rate rose to 6.7%, and manufacturing sales dropped 3.0% in January.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a concentrated negative activity cluster. Employment fell sharply for a second straight month, the unemployment rate rose, full-time employment dropped by 108,000, and private-sector employment fell for a second consecutive month. Youth employment also weakened materially, with youth unemployment rising to 14.1%. That is not just a soft headline; it is compositionally weak labour data.

The wage detail should not be over-read. Average hourly wages rose 3.9% y/y, but the tape is right to treat that cautiously because the job losses were concentrated enough in lower-paid segments to make composition distortion a live issue. Meanwhile, manufacturing sales fell 3.0% in January, broadly confirming that the prior day’s weak trade/wholesale batch was not a one-off external-sector anomaly. Capacity utilization at 78.5%, down from a revised 78.9% in Q3, reinforces a demand-constrained rather than overheating picture.

State transition: deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:

The prior DM live file had already begun to flag Canada as fragile, with softer labour and mixed growth data. The 12 March trade/wholesale release then weakened the January activity base. This 13 March cluster compounds that deterioration rather than initiating it. The important shift is from “fragile” to “more clearly weakening across multiple demand-sensitive channels.”

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This materially strengthens the case that the BoC is in hold mode because of demand weakness, not because inflation has been solved. The sequence now matters: weak trade, weak wholesale, weak manufacturing sales, and a very weak labour print all arrived before the next BoC meeting. That is enough to extinguish the hike tail even with oil acting as an inflation shock. For CAD and the front end, the dominant signal is softer domestic demand overwhelming any near-term hawkish inflation impulse.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Japan still looks relatively firmer on domestic activity, while the UK and Canada are softening on real-economy demand before the oil shock has fully fed through; Sweden remains low-inflation but not convincingly strong on labour or activity.
Why it matters: DM central banks face the same inflation-risk overlay from energy, but from very different domestic-growth starting points.
Implication: The common policy direction is not convergence, but differentiated prolonged hold.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

Canada Employment / Unemployment / Manufacturing Sales / Capacity Utilization (13 Mar 2026)
Why it matters: This is the strongest single negative DM cluster of the week. Labour, manufacturing, and utilization all point in the same direction: demand is weakening fast enough to shut the door on any BoC hike tail despite the oil shock.

United Kingdom GDP / Services / Industrial Output / Manufacturing / Trade (13 Mar 2026)
Why it matters: This was the cleanest “pre-shock stagnation” print in the file. The domestic activity side was weak, but trade improved sharply, which makes it analytically richer than a simple flat-GDP headline. It is central for BoE bind analysis.

Japan GDP Revised / Capex (10 Mar 2026)
Why it matters: This remains the key positive DM relative-growth surprise of the week. The capex-led upgrade still anchors the “Japan firmer than Europe/Canada” read and is the main counterweight to the broader DM softening pattern.

MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD
Demand: ~ Mixed
Supporting releases: UK retail strong; Australia GDP beat; Sweden GDP/retail improved; Canada Q4 GDP contracted; NZ card spending weak; Japan GDP soft.
Interpretation: DM demand held up in pockets, but the pattern was uneven across countries and sectors.

Labor: ~ Mixed / Softening
Supporting releases: UK labour softened; Sweden unemployment rose; Australia unemployment held at 4.1%; Japan unemployment edged up; Norway labour-force unemployment rose.
Interpretation: Labour is no longer uniformly tight across DM, but there is still no generalized break.

Inflation: ↓ Disinflation with sticky pockets
Supporting releases: UK headline CPI cooled but services stayed 4.4%; Canada CPI/core softened; Sweden CPIF stayed at target then softened on flash; Japan Tokyo/Nationwide CPI cooled; Australia core stayed sticky.
Interpretation: Disinflation continued, but UK services and Australian core measures still complicate clean-cut narratives.

Financial Conditions: ~ Restrictive
Supporting releases: UK housing subdued; Canada housing starts weak; Australia building approvals slumped; Norway credit eased.
Interpretation: Rate-sensitive sectors still show restrictive-policy transmission.

DATA QUALITY FLAG
Seasonal distortion risk: Medium
Revision sensitivity: Medium
Signal confidence: Moderate

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK
Demand improved in some hard-data releases while labour softened and inflation stayed sticky in key services/core pockets.
Implication:
DM is not in a clean recession regime, but neither is it in a clean re-acceleration regime. The main policy risk is softer labour/growth appearing before sticky services/core inflation is fully resolved.

MAJOR EVENTS — WEEK 2
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

TUESDAY 04 FEB — RELEASED

AUSTRALIA — RBA Interest Rate Decision (Feb meeting)
• Actual: 3.85% HIKE 25bp vs 3.60% vs Consensus: 3.85%
• Vote: UNANIMOUS (9-0)

PROFESSIONAL COMMENTARY (aggregated):
→ RBA Governor Michele Bullock: "Recent run of data gives Board clear-enough view that underlying momentum of inflation is too strong. We updated assessment/outlook and concluded rate no longer at right level to get inflation back to target in reasonable timeframe. Based on data, Board thinks it will take longer for inflation to return to target - this is NOT acceptable outcome."
→ Bullock on Q4 inflation: "Inflation in Q4 was 'materially higher' than expected, partly due to less persistent factors. Private demand growth much stronger than expected in H2 2025. Economy judged further from balance than assumed, growing above potential. Some indicators suggest financial conditions may now be 'somewhat accommodative'."
→ Bullock on forward path: "Cannot allow inflation to get away from us again. Did not discuss 50bp rise. Board will be cautious on rates. Seeing some tightening in financial conditions through A$. Rising A$ helpful at margin. Do not know if this will be tightening cycle. Can't rule anything in or out. Board will actively monitor data."
→ RBA forecasts (Statement on Monetary Policy): "Assumes cash rate 3.9% by June, 4.2% by Dec (implies 1-2 more hikes). Trimmed mean inflation 3.7% Q2, 3.2% Q4 2026, 2.7% Q4 2027. GDP growth 2.3% Q4 2025, 1.8% Q4 2026. Unemployment 4.3% Q4 2026."
→ Westpac Economist: "RBA forecasts imply quarterly trimmed mean ~0.9% for next two quarters before reverting to 0.7%. At 3.2% annual trimmed mean still above target at end-2026. Beyond mid-year, temporary elements expected to unwind and restrictive policy to dampen inflation, returning close to mid-point 2.5% by mid-2028."
→ NAB Chief Economist Sally Auld: "Another hike likely in May. Risk of March hike or 50bp increase."
→ Markets: "AUD/USD retook 0.7000, up 0.75% on decision. 3yr yields jumped 8bp to 4.30%. Investors price ~75% probability follow-up May hike. Markets expect additional 40bp tightening total this year."

COMMENT (HF sensitivity):
Dominant signal: RBA DELIVERED hike as expected, but FORWARD GUIDANCE is more hawkish than market anticipated. The CRITICAL shift: RBA's own forecasts NOW ASSUME cash rate 4.2% by Dec 2026 (vs 3.85% current) = implies 1-2 MORE hikes baked into their inflation path. Bullock's "underlying momentum too strong" + "not acceptable" timeframe language = Board COMMITTED to further tightening unless inflation cooperates immediately. Combined with UNANIMOUS vote (no dissent, vs some prior meetings had 1-2 dissenters), this signals RBA Board is FULLY ALIGNED on hawkish path.

Secondary: The RBA's assessment that "financial conditions somewhat accommodative" despite cash rate 3.60% (pre-hike) is REGIME-DEFINING. This means: (a) Credit growth too strong ("picked up sharply"), (b) AUD appreciation helping but not enough, (c) Housing market "continuing to pick up", (d) Private demand "strengthened substantially more than expected". All point to economy OVERHEATING not cooling, which justifies ongoing tightening.

Noise: Bullock's "didn't discuss 50bp" comment is somewhat reassuring (no panic mode), but doesn't change base case of 2-3 more 25bp hikes

Reaction-function impact (RBA): **HIKING CYCLE CONFIRMED.** Forward path: March HOLD (assess Feb hike transmission), May HIKE 25bp to 4.10%, August possible 3rd hike to 4.35%, then PAUSE. Terminal rate 4.10-4.35% (vs prior market expectations ~3.85% one-and-done). Triggers for MORE hikes: (a) Q1 trimmed mean >0.9% QQ (annualizes >3.6%), (b) Unemployment <4.0% (tightening not loosening), (c) Private credit growth >6.0% YY (currently elevated). Triggers for PAUSE after Feb: (a) Q1 trimmed mean <0.6% QQ (disinflation resuming), (b) Unemployment >4.5% (labor market cracking), (c) Housing prices falling >5% (wealth effect reversing).

Transmission: **AUD STRONGEST G10 CURRENCY.** RBA is ONLY major DM central bank in hiking cycle (Fed holding 4.50% but NOT hiking further; BoJ paused 0.75%; BoE/BoC/Riksbank/SNB cutting). AUD/USD 0.70 → 0.72-0.75 by Q2 2026 if RBA delivers 2-3 hikes as expected. AUD/JPY especially explosive (RBA hiking vs BoJ paused): 100 → 108-112. AUD/CAD strong (RBA hiking vs BoC cutting): current ~1.00 → 1.05-1.08. Australia rates: 3yr swap 4.30% → 4.60-4.80% (pricing full hiking cycle). 10yr bond 4.50% → 4.70-4.90% (term premium rising as RBA tightens while Fed holds). Australia equity (ASX200) VULNERABLE near-term: (a) Banks benefit (NIM expansion) but offset by (b) REITs/utilities hurt (higher discount rates), (c) Consumer discretionary hurt (rate hikes dampen spending), (d) Resources supported (commodity boom + China stimulus). Net effect: ASX range-bound, underperform global equities.

Regime comparison: Australia is now the ANTI-CONSENSUS DM economy - hiking while world cuts/pauses, domestic demand booming while others stagnating, inflation rising while others disinflating. This creates **MAXIMUM G10 FX DISPERSION** and makes AUD the cleanest "DM carry + growth + commodity" triple-play trade.

THURSDAY 29 JAN — RELEASED

AUSTRALIA — CPI Q4 2025
• Headline: 3.8% YY (Monthly Dec: 3.8% vs 3.4% Nov)
• Trimmed Mean: 0.9% QQ vs 0.8% Consensus, 3.4% YY vs 3.2% prior
• All core measures BEAT consensus

PROFESSIONAL COMMENTARY (aggregated from public sources):
→ RBA Governor Michele Bullock (Feb 4 Week 2 press conference, provides context): "Inflation in Q4 was 'materially higher' than expected. Private demand growth much stronger than expected in H2 2025. Economy judged to be further from balance than assumed, growing above potential. Some indicators suggest financial conditions may now be 'somewhat accommodative'. Total credit growth has picked up sharply, cash rate below some measures of neutral."
→ FXStreet (Feb 4 context): "December monthly CPI leaped to 3.8% from 3.4% in November, above forecasts of 3.6%. Trimmed mean CPI rose 0.9% quarterly in Q4, beating market forecasts of 0.8%. Following hot inflation, money markets implied 73% probability of rate hike vs 60% previously. Australia's big four banks (ANZ, Westpac, CBA, NAB) altered call, forecasting 25bp RBA rate hike."
→ CBA Chief Economist Luke Yeaman (Nov 2025 context): "Inflation surprised us all in Sep quarter - trimmed mean jumped 1.0% quarterly. We have lifted trimmed mean forecast for Q4 to 0.8% (from 0.6%). This is one key reason we don't expect rate cuts near-term. Even if not at full capacity, annual trimmed mean will be too high for RBA Board to be comfortable cutting."
→ NAB Economist (context): "RBA's preference for trimmed mean inflation has long history. Trimmed means tend to outperform headline and exclusion-based 'core' measures on range of criteria. RBA's unusual focus on trimmed mean reflects this."

COMMENT (HF sensitivity):
Dominant signal: Australia trimmed mean 0.9% QQ vs 0.8% consensus = BEAT on RBA's PRIMARY INFLATION GAUGE. This is CRITICAL because: (a) RBA explicitly targets trimmed mean (not headline), (b) 0.9% QQ annualizes to 3.6%+ (well above 2-3% target range midpoint 2.5%), (c) Governor Bullock WARNED in Dec "if data suggests inflation not slowing, will be considered at Feb board meeting" → this IS that data. Combined with Bullock's Feb 4 assessment that Q4 inflation "materially higher than expected" + economy "further from balance" + "financial conditions somewhat accommodative" + "credit growth picked up sharply" = HIKE CERTAIN.

Secondary: ALL core measures beat (not just trimmed mean) - monthly CPI 3.8% vs 3.4% prior + 3.6% consensus, confirming broadbased inflation persistence. This is NOT energy/food volatility, it's domestic demand overheating (CBA: "private demand growth much stronger than expected H2 2025").

Noise: Q4 includes some seasonal patterns, but 0.9% vs 0.8% beat is meaningful not statistical

Reaction-function impact (RBA): Feb 4 hike 25bp to 3.85% from 3.60% is CERTAIN (occurred Week 2 - confirmed by Bullock statement). Forward path: RBA will likely hike AGAIN in March/April if: (a) Q1 2026 trimmed mean >0.8% QQ, (b) Labor market stays tight (unemployment <4.3%), (c) Credit growth continues (already "picked up sharply"). Terminal rate likely 4.10-4.35% (2-3 more 25bp hikes) before pause. This makes RBA THE ONLY MAJOR DM CENTRAL BANK HIKING in early 2026 (Fed holding 4.50%, ECB cutting to 2.00%, BoE cutting to 3.75%, BoJ paused 0.75%, BoC cutting to 2.25%).

Transmission: AUD STRONGEST DM FX on fundamentals - only central bank hiking + commodity support (iron ore, LNG) + China stimulus tailwind. AUD/USD 0.65 → 0.68-0.70 by Q2 2026 if RBA hikes 2-3 times as expected. AUD/JPY especially strong (RBA hiking vs BoJ paused). Australia rates: 3yr swap likely 3.60% → 4.00%+ as markets price hiking cycle. FRIDAY 30 JAN — RELEASED

JAPAN — Tokyo CPI (Jan, leading indicator for national)
• Headline: 2.0% YY vs 2.2% Consensus vs 2.4% Prior (Dec)
• Core (ex-fresh food): 2.3% vs 2.5% prior
• Core-core (ex-fresh food & energy): 2.6% vs 2.8% prior

PROFESSIONAL COMMENTARY (aggregated from public sources):
→ BoJ Outlook Report (Jan 23): "Year-on-year CPI (ex-fresh food) likely to decelerate to level below 2% in H1 2026, with waning effects of rise in food prices (rice) and partly due to government measures to address rising prices. However, mechanism in which wages and prices rise moderately in interaction will be maintained, underlying CPI inflation will continue rising moderately."
→ ING Economist: "Headline inflation eased to 2.0% in Tokyo December, below expectations (vs 2.7% Nov, 2.3% consensus), mainly due to renewed energy subsidies, stable rice prices, low petroleum costs. These factors expected to keep inflation low into 2026. Core inflation ex-fresh food & energy slowed to 2.6% (vs 2.8% Nov). With headline expected to drop below 2.0% and core easing toward 2.0%, BoJ likely to carefully assess impact of previous rate hikes before considering further hikes."
→ BoJ Governor Ueda (Dec 19 context): "BoJ would need to check impact of rate change each time before moving again." Signals data-dependent approach, no preset hiking path.
→ ING Forecast: "We expect BoJ's next rate hike in October 2026 (not Q1/Q2). However, if JPY weakens and drives up import prices, timeline could be moved forward to Q2 2026."
→ CNBC Analysis: "Tokyo CPI 2.0% is AT BoJ's 2% target, lowest since March 2022. Core-core 2.9% down from 3.0% is key - this strips food/energy volatility. Food inflation fell to 5.1% from 6.1% (13-month low), driven by slowest rice price rise in 16 months."

COMMENT (HF sensitivity):
Dominant signal: Tokyo CPI 2.0% = AT BOJ TARGET for first time since March 2022, and 20bp BELOW consensus (2.2%). This is a MAJOR DISINFLATION SURPRISE that removes urgency for BoJ to hike near-term. CRITICAL: Tokyo is leading indicator for national CPI (released ~1 week earlier), so Tokyo 2.0% signals national CPI will also fall toward/below 2.0% in Jan. Combined with BoJ's own forecast ("CPI likely below 2% H1 2026 due to waning rice prices + government energy subsidies"), this confirms BoJ PAUSE extends through Q1 2026 minimum.

Secondary: Core-core (ex-fresh food & energy) 2.6% vs 2.8% prior is the KEY measure for underlying inflation - this strips volatile components and shows TRUE demand-driven inflation. 2.6% is still ABOVE 2.0% target but FALLING (from 2.8%), which suggests "wages-prices interaction" (BoJ's goal for sustainable inflation) is WEAKENING not strengthening. This contradicts BoJ's hope that "wage-price loop will be maintained" and argues for LONGER pause.

Noise: January seasonal effects (energy subsidies renewed, rice prices seasonal), but magnitude of miss (2.0% vs 2.2% consensus) is meaningful

Reaction-function impact (BoJ): Next hike pushed to Q4 2026 (October per ING forecast) from prior market expectations of Q1-Q2 2026. BoJ will need to see: (a) Headline CPI ABOVE 2.0% for sustained period (not below), (b) Core-core ABOVE 2.5% (wage-price loop accelerating not decelerating), (c) Spring wage negotiations (shunto) deliver 5%+ raises (major unions targeting this, but delivery uncertain), (d) JPY stability (if JPY weakens to >155 vs USD, import price pressure could force earlier hike). Terminal rate likely 1.00-1.25% by end-2027 (from current 0.75%), very gradual path.

Transmission: JPY WEAKENING pressure near-term (BoJ pause extends while Fed holding 4.50% = rate differential persists). JPY likely 152-157 range vs USD in Q1 2026 (from current ~155). If BoJ signals "no hike until October" explicitly, JPY could test 160+ (intervention risk from MoF at that level). Japan equity (Nikkei/Topix) SUPPORTED by: (a) BoJ pause = easy financial conditions, (b) JPY weakness = export competitiveness, (c) Wage growth (even if 5%+ not achieved, 3-4% still solid), but VULNERABLE to: (d) Global slowdown (export demand), (e) China weakness (trade channel).

Regime comparison: Japan vs Australia is MAXIMUM DM MONETARY DIVERGENCE - RBA hiking to 3.85%+ (inflation 3.4% trimmed mean above target) while BoJ pausing at 0.75% (inflation 2.0% at target falling). AUD/JPY is cleanest DM carry trade: long AUD (hiking + commodity support) vs short JPY (paused + weak). AUD/JPY 99 → 105-108 by Q2 2026.

Transmission: CAD weakness confirmed - growth stalling + BoC cutting = double negative for CAD

CROSS-DM SYNTHESIS — WEEK 1
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

THEME: DM CENTRAL BANK DIVERGENCE AT MAXIMUM

**THE RBA IS ALONE:**
Australia is the ONLY major DM central bank HIKING in early 2026. This is unprecedented divergence:
- RBA: Hiking from 3.60% → 3.85% (Week 2) → likely 4.10-4.35% terminal
- Fed: HOLDING 4.50% (no cuts until H2 2026 if at all)
- ECB: CUTTING 3.00% → 2.00% (Week 2 EUR data)
- BoE: GRADUAL CUTS 3.75% → terminal 3.00-3.25%
- BoJ: PAUSED 0.75% (next hike Oct 2026)
- BoC: CUTTING 2.25% → terminal 1.50-1.75%
- Riksbank: AGGRESSIVE CUTS 2.25% → terminal 1.50%
- SNB: DOVISH (growth concerns)
- RBNZ: CUTTING (growth weak)

**WHY IS AUSTRALIA DIFFERENT?**
1. **Inflation persistence:** Trimmed mean 3.4% YY (Q4 0.9% QQ annualizes to 3.6%+) vs other DM at/near target
2. **Demand overheating:** CBA assessment "private demand growth much stronger than expected H2 2025" + "credit growth picked up sharply"
3. **Labor tightness:** Unemployment ~4.3% (near full employment) vs Canada 6.5%, Sweden higher, UK higher
4. **Commodity boom:** Iron ore, LNG exports strong (China stimulus + global energy demand)
5. **Financial conditions:** Bullock: "Some indicators suggest financial conditions somewhat accommodative" despite cash rate 3.60% = economy OVERHEATING not cooling

**DM FX HIERARCHY (Week 1 snapshot):**

**STRONGEST (Appreciation):**
→ **AUD (HIGHEST):** RBA solo hawk (hiking 3.85%+), commodity support, China tailwind, growth strong
→ **CHF:** SNB dovish but safe-haven demand, EUR weakness supports CHF
→ **JPY:** BoJ paused but safe-haven bid (risk-off episodes), intervention support at 160 vs USD

**NEUTRAL (Range-bound):**
→ **GBP:** BoE gradual cuts, growth soft landing, rates 3.75% still restrictive
→ **NZD:** RBNZ cutting but less aggressive than Sweden/Canada

**WEAK (Depreciation):**
→ **CAD:** BoC cutting aggressively (terminal 1.50-1.75%), growth stalling (GDP 0.0%), rate differential vs Fed widening
→ **SEK:** Riksbank aggressive cuts (terminal 1.50%), growth miss (Q4 0.2% vs 0.6%)
→ **NOK:** Norges cutting, oil support mitigates but still dovish

**DM RATES DIVERGENCE:**

**HIKING:**
- Australia 3yr swap: 3.20% → 4.00%+ (pricing 2-3 more hikes)

**HOLDING:**
- US 2yr: 4.20-4.40% (Fed terminal debate)
- Japan 2yr: 0.60-0.80% (BoJ Oct hike priced, gradual)

**CUTTING:**
- Canada 2yr: 2.60% → 1.80-2.00% (pricing 3-4 more cuts)
- Sweden 2yr: 2.00% → 1.40-1.60% (aggressive easing)
- UK 2yr: 4.00% → 3.20-3.50% (gradual BoE cuts)
- EUR 2yr (Sch Bloomberg): 2.20% → 1.80-2.00% (ECB cutting)

**CROSS-DM TRADES IMPLIED:**

**HIGHEST CONVICTION:**
1. **Long AUD / Short CAD:** "Solo hawk vs aggressive dove" — RBA hiking to 4.10%+ vs BoC cutting to 1.50-1.75%, commodity divergence (Aus iron ore strong vs Canada oil weaker), growth divergence (Aus strong vs Canada stalling)
2. **Long AUD / Short JPY:** "Hike vs pause" — maximum DM rate differential widening, cleanest carry trade, AUD/JPY 99 → 105-108
3. **Long AUD / Short SEK:** "Strong vs weak" — RBA hiking vs Riksbank aggressive cuts, growth divergence (Aus overheating vs Sweden stalling)

**MEDIUM CONVICTION:**
4. **Long GBP / Short CAD:** "Gradual vs aggressive easing" — BoE measured cuts vs BoC frontloading, UK soft landing vs Canada stall
5. **Long CHF / Short SEK:** "Safe-haven vs peripheral" — SNB dovish but CHF supported, Riksbank aggressive cuts

**AVOID:**
- Short AUD (fundamentals too strong, RBA hiking)
- Long CAD (BoC cutting + growth stalling = double negative)
- Long SEK (Riksbank aggressive + growth weak)

**REGIME SHIFT SUMMARY:**

**PRIOR REGIME (Pre-Week 1):**
"DM central banks mostly dovish/neutral; Australia/NZ restrictive bias; BoJ gradual normalization; BoE/BoC/Riksbank/SNB cutting"

**UPDATED REGIME (Post-Week 1):**
**"DM DIVERGENCE AT MAXIMUM — RBA SOLO HAWK (trimmed mean 0.9% QQ → hike Feb 4 certain, more coming); BoJ PAUSE EXTENDS (Tokyo CPI 2.0% at target → next hike Oct 2026); BoC/Riksbank AGGRESSIVE DOVES (growth stalling, terminal rates sub-2.0%); BoE GRADUAL (soft landing path); AUD STRONGEST DM FX (only hiker + commodity boom + China tailwind); CAD/SEK WEAKEST (aggressive cutting + growth weak)."**

**REGIME TRIGGERS:**

**BULLISH (DM FX convergence, AUD weakens):**
→ **Australia CPI COLLAPSE:** Q1 trimmed mean <0.5% QQ (vs 0.9% Q4) = RBA forced to pause/reverse, AUD crashes
→ **China HARD LANDING:** PMI <47 for 3 months, commodity crash (iron ore $80 → $60) = AUD loses commodity support
→ **Fed DOVISH PIVOT:** 50bp March cut + "growth concerns" = USD weakens broadly, rate differentials compress

**BEARISH (DM FX divergence widens, AUD strengthens further):**
→ **Australia LABOR TIGHTENS:** Unemployment <4.0% + wages >4.5% = RBA forced into 50bp hikes or faster pace (terminal 4.50%+)
→ **Canada RECESSION:** GDP negative Q1 + unemployment >7.0% = BoC cuts 50bp (panic mode), CAD collapses
→ **BoJ DELAYS FURTHER:** Explicitly signals "no hike until 2027" = JPY to 165+, AUD/JPY to 110+

**NEUTRAL (Status quo):**
→ **Gradual divergence persists:** RBA hikes 2-3 times to 4.10-4.35% (as expected), BoJ hikes Oct 2026 (as expected), BoC cuts to 1.75% (as expected) = Current FX hierarchy stable through H1 2026

BOTTOM LINE — WEEK SYNTHESIS (what’s new / what mattered)

UK: “soft-landing” probability rose sharply — and the BoE cutting narrative got less urgent.
The week started with mixed labour/wages (not weak enough to force cuts), then CPI delivered “no re-acceleration in core/services,” but the key narrative shock was Friday’s UK flash PMIs + retail sales: that combo is exactly what makes the BoE’s reaction function asymmetric (they can ease only if growth cooperates and inflation keeps falling). This week delivered growth cooperation. Net: cuts become later / more conditional rather than “imminent”; GBP support becomes more durable because it’s backed by activity momentum, not just positioning.

Japan: BoJ stayed on rails — the economy still sends mixed signals, so the tightening path stays ultra-gradual.
Hard data (machinery orders, trade balance) argued for caution; surveys (PMIs) argued for resilience. CPI core in line and the policy decision unchanged crystallise the baseline: BoJ is not accelerating the hiking cycle; it’s waiting for sustained, high-confidence confirmation. Net: JPY remains more a function of global rates and risk than of domestic policy surprise.

Australia/New Zealand: the “rates stay restrictive” tail got fatter (for different reasons).
Australia printed a growth/labour/PMI cluster that keeps the RBA constrained (even if inflation is improving elsewhere). New Zealand CPI came in firmer, pushing easing optionality out. Net: AUD/NZD sensitivity tilts back toward front-end repricing rather than pure risk beta.

Canada: cross-currents (disinflation + resilient consumption) keep the BoC in patient mode, not forced either way.
BoC core measures were consistent with disinflation continuing, but retail sales were strong enough to prevent a “growth break” narrative. Net: BoC can ease, but the urgency is not accelerating — market should price a gradual path, not a rush.

What did not matter this week: low-frequency confidence/sentiment (GfK), stable policy holds (Norges/BoJ were expected), and single-point activity prints without confirmation (one-offs only mattered when clustered, like UK Friday).

Australia — CPI (Nov)
Headline m/m: 0.00% vs 0.30% consensus vs 0.00% prior
Headline y/y: 3.40% vs 3.70% consensus vs 3.80% prior
Trimmed mean m/m (SA): 0.30% vs 0.25% consensus vs 0.30% prior

Dominant signal: no clean February signal — headline disinflation is clear, but underlying is still in a “grey zone” (trimmed mean momentum not collapsing).
Secondary: trimmed mean deceleration (key ref: ~0.258% m/m on the trimmed mean) still leaves upside risk to the quarterly CPI forecast (~0.8% q/q); housing details are mixed (rents firmer, new dwellings softer), while discretionary services (restaurants/takeaways) look contained.
Noise: monthly CPI is composition-heavy and can mislead; the quarterly aggregate is the decision-grade input.
Reaction function: keeps both hike/hold on the table; the bar for a hike is not met by this print alone, but the bar for a “done” declaration isn’t met either.
Transmission: caps AUD directionality via front-end range-trading — policy debate stays live until the full-quarter CPI arrives.

Secondary: housing activity may stabilise sooner than expected.
Noise: catch-up volatility.
Reaction function: offsets some dovish CPI impulse but clearly secondary.
Transmission: marginal support to growth expectations, but inflation dominates.

THURSDAY 8 — RELEASED

Australia — Balance on Goods (Nov)
Actual A$2,936m vs A$4,900m consensus vs A$4,385m prior
Dominant signal: external surplus underwhelms materially; net trade impulse is weaker than expected into Q4 tracking.
Secondary: consistent with a softer export pulse and/or resilience in import demand; confirms AUD’s sensitivity to terms-of-trade and China-linked volume risk.
Noise: one month can swing with commodity pricing/shipments.
Reaction function: not an RBA trigger by itself, but it removes one “growth cushion” that would have made hawkishness easier to justify after the CPI grey zone.
Transmission: mildly AUD-negative through current-account/terms-of-trade optics; reduces upside convexity.

Australia — Exports (Nov)
Actual -2.9% vs +3.4% consensus vs +4.3% prior
Dominant signal: export momentum rolled over hard vs expectations.
Secondary: makes the weak trade balance more “real” (not just imports noise).
Reaction function: dovish-tilt at the margin via growth impulse, but secondary to quarterly CPI.
Transmission: AUD-negative via growth/earnings channel.

Australia — Imports (Nov)

Noise: shipment timing.

Transmission: limited.
